The\r\nunreasonable nimbus of romance with which she had encircled that man\r\nmight be her misery. How could she allow herself to become so\r\ninfatuated with a stranger? And to fill the cup of her sorrow there\r\nwould be Thomasin, living day after day in inflammable proximity to\r\nhim; for she had just learnt that, contrary to her first belief, he was\r\ngoing to stay at home some considerable time.\r\n\r\nShe reached the wicket at Mistover Knap, but before opening it she\r\nturned and faced the heath once more. The form of Rainbarrow stood\r\nabove the hills, and the moon stood above Rainbarrow. The air was\r\ncharged with silence and frost. The scene reminded Eustacia of a\r\ncircumstance which till that moment she had totally forgotten. She had\r\npromised to meet Wildeve by the Barrow this very night at eight, to\r\ngive a final answer to his pleading for an elopement.\r\n\r\nShe herself had fixed the evening and the hour. He had probably come to\r\nthe spot, waited there in the cold, and been greatly disappointed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, so much the better\xe2\x80\x94it did not hurt him,\xe2\x80\x9d she said serenely.\r\nWildeve had at present the rayless outline of the sun through smoked\r\nglass, and she could say such things as that with the greatest\r\nfacility.\r\n\r\nShe remained deeply pondering; and Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s winning manner towards\r\nher cousin arose again upon Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO that she had been married to Damon before this!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd she\r\nwould if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for me! If I had only known\xe2\x80\x94if I had only\r\nknown!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia once more lifted her deep stormy eyes to the moonlight, and,\r\nsighing that tragic sigh of hers which was so much like a shudder,\r\nentered the shadow of the roof. She threw off her trappings in the\r\nouthouse, rolled them up, and went indoors to her chamber.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII.\r\nA Coalition between Beauty and Oddness\r\n\r\n\r\nThe old captain\xe2\x80\x99s prevailing indifference to his granddaughter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmovements left her free as a bird to follow her own courses; but it so\r\nhappened that he did take upon himself the next morning to ask her why\r\nshe had walked out so late.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly in search of events, Grandfather,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking out of the\r\nwindow with that drowsy latency of manner which discovered so much\r\nforce behind it whenever the trigger was pressed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSearch of events\xe2\x80\x94one would think you were one of the bucks I knew at\r\none-and-twenty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is lonely here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the better. If I were living in a town my whole time would be\r\ntaken up in looking after you. I fully expected you would have been\r\nhome when I returned from the Woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t conceal what I did. I wanted an adventure, and I went with the\r\nmummers. I played the part of the Turkish Knight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, never? Ha, ha! Good gad! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect it of you, Eustacia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my first performance, and it certainly will be my last. Now I\r\nhave told you\xe2\x80\x94and remember it is a secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course. But, Eustacia, you never did\xe2\x80\x94ha! ha! Dammy, how \xe2\x80\x99twould\r\nhave pleased me forty years ago! But remember, no more of it, my girl.\r\nYou may walk on the heath night or day, as you choose, so that you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t bother me; but no figuring in breeches again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou need have no fear for me, Grandpapa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the conversation ceased, Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s moral training never exceeding\r\nin severity a dialogue of this sort, which, if it ever became\r\nprofitable to good works, would be a result not dear at the price. But\r\nher thoughts soon strayed far from her own personality; and, full of a\r\npassionate and indescribable solicitude for one to whom she was not\r\neven a name, she went forth into the amplitude of tanned wild around\r\nher, restless as Ahasuerus the Jew. She was about half a mile from her\r\nresidence when she beheld a sinister redness arising from a ravine a\r\nlittle way in advance\xe2\x80\x94dull and lurid like a flame in sunlight and she\r\nguessed it to signify Diggory Venn.\r\n\r\nWhen the farmers who had wished to buy in a new stock of reddle during\r\nthe last month had inquired where Venn was to be found, people replied,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn Egdon Heath.\xe2\x80\x9d Day after day the answer was the same. Now, since\r\nEgdon was populated with heath-croppers and furze-cutters rather than\r\nwith sheep and shepherds, and the downs where most of the latter were\r\nto be found lay some to the north, some to the west of Egdon, his\r\nreason for camping about there like Israel in Zin was not apparent. The\r\nposition was central and occasionally desirable. But the sale of reddle\r\nwas not Diggory\xe2\x80\x99s primary object in remaining on the heath,\r\nparticularly at so late a period of the year, when most travellers of\r\nhis class had gone into winter quarters.\r\n\r\nEustacia looked at the lonely man. Wildeve had told her at their last\r\nmeeting that Venn had been thrust forward by Mrs. Yeobright as one\r\nready and anxious to take his place as Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s betrothed. His figure\r\nwas perfect, his face young and well outlined, his eye bright, his\r\nintelligence keen, and his position one which he could readily better\r\nif he chose. But in spite of possibilities it was not likely that\r\nThomasin would accept this Ishmaelitish creature while she had a cousin\r\nlike Yeobright at her elbow, and Wildeve at the same time not\r\nabsolutely indifferent. Eustacia was not long in guessing that poor\r\nMrs. Yeobright, in her anxiety for her niece\xe2\x80\x99s future, had mentioned\r\nthis lover to stimulate the zeal of the other. Eustacia was on the side\r\nof the Yeobrights now, and entered into the spirit of the aunt\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndesire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, miss,\xe2\x80\x9d said the reddleman, taking off his cap of\r\nhareskin, and apparently bearing her no ill-will from recollection of\r\ntheir last meeting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, reddleman,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, hardly troubling to lift her\r\nheavily shaded eyes to his. \xe2\x80\x9cI did not know you were so near. Is your\r\nvan here too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVenn moved his elbow towards a hollow in which a dense brake of\r\npurple-stemmed brambles had grown to such vast dimensions as almost to\r\nform a dell. Brambles, though churlish when handled, are kindly shelter\r\nin early winter, being the latest of the deciduous bushes to lose their\r\nleaves.\r\n\r\nThe roof and chimney of Venn\xe2\x80\x99s caravan showed behind the tracery and\r\ntangles of the brake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remain near this part?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked with more interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have business here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot altogether the selling of reddle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has nothing to do with that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has to do with Miss Yeobright?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer face seemed to ask for an armed peace, and he therefore said\r\nfrankly, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, miss; it is on account of her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn account of your approaching marriage with her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVenn flushed through his stain. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make sport of me, Miss Vye,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t true?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was thus convinced that the reddleman was a mere _pis aller_ in\r\nMrs. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s mind; one, moreover, who had not even been informed of\r\nhis promotion to that lowly standing. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a mere notion of mine,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said quietly; and was about to pass by without further speech,\r\nwhen, looking round to the right, she saw a painfully well-known figure\r\nserpentining upwards by one of the little paths which led to the top\r\nwhere she stood. Owing to the necessary windings of his course his back\r\nwas at present towards them. She glanced quickly round; to escape that\r\nman there was only one way. Turning to Venn, she said, \xe2\x80\x9cWould you allow\r\nme to rest a few minutes in your van? The banks are damp for sitting\r\non.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, miss; I\xe2\x80\x99ll make a place for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe followed him behind the dell of brambles to his wheeled dwelling\r\ninto which Venn mounted, placing the three-legged stool just within the\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the best I can do for you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, stepping down and\r\nretiring to the path, where he resumed the smoking of his pipe as he\r\nwalked up and down.\r\n\r\nEustacia bounded into the vehicle and sat on the stool, ensconced from\r\nview on the side towards the trackway. Soon she heard the brushing of\r\nother feet than the reddleman\xe2\x80\x99s, a not very friendly \xe2\x80\x9cGood day\xe2\x80\x9d uttered\r\nby two men in passing each other, and then the dwindling of the\r\nfoot-fall of one of them in a direction onwards. Eustacia stretched her\r\nneck forward till she caught a glimpse of a receding back and\r\nshoulders; and she felt a wretched twinge of misery, she knew not why.\r\nIt was the sickening feeling which, if the changed heart has any\r\ngenerosity at all in its composition, accompanies the sudden sight of a\r\nonce-loved one who is beloved no more.\r\n\r\nWhen Eustacia descended to proceed on her way the reddleman came near.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was Mr. Wildeve who passed, miss,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly, and expressed\r\nby his face that he expected her to feel vexed at having been sitting\r\nunseen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I saw him coming up the hill,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Eustacia. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you\r\ntell me that?\xe2\x80\x9d It was a bold question, considering the reddleman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nknowledge of her past love; but her undemonstrative manner had power to\r\nrepress the opinions of those she treated as remote from her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear that you can ask it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the reddleman bluntly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, now I think of it, it agrees with what I saw last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94what was that?\xe2\x80\x9d Eustacia wished to leave him, but wished to know.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Wildeve stayed at Rainbarrow a long time waiting for a lady who\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou waited too, it seems?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I always do. I was glad to see him disappointed. He will be there\r\nagain tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be again disappointed. The truth is, reddleman, that that lady, so\r\nfar from wishing to stand in the way of Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s marriage with Mr.\r\nWildeve, would be very glad to promote it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVenn felt much astonishment at this avowal, though he did not show it\r\nclearly; that exhibition may greet remarks which are one remove from\r\nexpectation, but it is usually withheld in complicated cases of two\r\nremoves and upwards. \xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, miss,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know that Mr. Wildeve will come to Rainbarrow again\r\ntonight?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard him say to himself that he would. He\xe2\x80\x99s in a regular temper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia looked for a moment what she felt, and she murmured, lifting\r\nher deep dark eyes anxiously to his, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish I knew what to do. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to be uncivil to him; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to see him again; and I\r\nhave some few little things to return to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you choose to send \xe2\x80\x99em by me, miss, and a note to tell him that you\r\nwish to say no more to him, I\xe2\x80\x99ll take it for you quite privately. That\r\nwould be the most straightforward way of letting him know your mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustacia. \xe2\x80\x9cCome towards my house, and I will bring it\r\nout to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went on, and as the path was an infinitely small parting in the\r\nshaggy locks of the heath, the reddleman followed exactly in her trail.\r\nShe saw from a distance that the captain was on the bank sweeping the\r\nhorizon with his telescope; and bidding Venn to wait where he stood she\r\nentered the house alone.\r\n\r\nIn ten minutes she returned with a parcel and a note, and said, in\r\nplacing them in his hand, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you so ready to take these for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you ask that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you think to serve Thomasin in some way by it. Are you as\r\nanxious as ever to help on her marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVenn was a little moved. \xe2\x80\x9cI would sooner have married her myself,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid in a low voice. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what I feel is that if she cannot be happy\r\nwithout him I will do my duty in helping her to get him, as a man\r\nought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia looked curiously at the singular man who spoke thus. What a\r\nstrange sort of love, to be entirely free from that quality of\r\nselfishness which is frequently the chief constituent of the passion,\r\nand sometimes its only one! The reddleman\xe2\x80\x99s disinterestedness was so\r\nwell deserving of respect that it overshot respect by being barely\r\ncomprehended; and she almost thought it absurd.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we are both of one mind at last,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Venn gloomily. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if you would tell me, miss, why you\r\ntake such an interest in her, I should be easier. It is so sudden and\r\nstrange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia appeared at a loss. \xe2\x80\x9cI cannot tell you that, reddleman,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid coldly.\r\n\r\nVenn said no more. He pocketed the letter, and, bowing to Eustacia,\r\nwent away.\r\n\r\nRainbarrow had again become blended with night when Wildeve ascended\r\nthe long acclivity at its base. On his reaching the top a shape grew up\r\nfrom the earth immediately behind him. It was that of Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nemissary. He slapped Wildeve on the shoulder. The feverish young\r\ninn-keeper and ex-engineer started like Satan at the touch of\r\nIthuriel\xe2\x80\x99s spear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe meeting is always at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, at this place,\xe2\x80\x9d said Venn,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand here we are\xe2\x80\x94we three.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe three?\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve, looking quickly round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; you, and I, and she. This is she.\xe2\x80\x9d He held up the letter and\r\nparcel.\r\n\r\nWildeve took them wonderingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite see what this means,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you come here? There must be some mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be cleared from your mind when you have read the letter.\r\nLanterns for one.\xe2\x80\x9d The reddleman struck a light, kindled an inch of\r\ntallow-candle which he had brought, and sheltered it with his cap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve, discerning by the candle-light an obscure\r\nrubicundity of person in his companion. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are the reddleman I saw on\r\nthe hill this morning\xe2\x80\x94why, you are the man who\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease read the letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you had come from the other one I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been surprised,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nmurmured Wildeve as he opened the letter and read. His face grew\r\nserious.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Mr. WILDEVE.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter some thought I have decided once and for all that we must hold\r\nno further communication. The more I consider the matter the more I am\r\nconvinced that there must be an end to our acquaintance. Had you been\r\nuniformly faithful to me throughout these two years you might now have\r\nsome ground for accusing me of heartlessness; but if you calmly\r\nconsider what I bore during the period of your desertion, and how I\r\npassively put up with your courtship of another without once\r\ninterfering, you will, I think, own that I have a right to consult my\r\nown feelings when you come back to me again. That these are not what\r\nthey were towards you may, perhaps, be a fault in me, but it is one\r\nwhich you can scarcely reproach me for when you remember how you left\r\nme for Thomasin.\r\n    The little articles you gave me in the early part of our friendship\r\n    are returned by the bearer of this letter. They should rightly have\r\n    been sent back when I first heard of your engagement to her.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEUSTACIA.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nBy the time that Wildeve reached her name the blankness with which he\r\nhad read the first half of the letter intensified to mortification. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nam made a great fool of, one way and another,\xe2\x80\x9d he said pettishly. \xe2\x80\x9cDo\r\nyou know what is in this letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe reddleman hummed a tune.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you answer me?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Wildeve warmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRu-um-tum-tum,\xe2\x80\x9d sang the reddleman.\r\n\r\nWildeve stood looking on the ground beside Venn\xe2\x80\x99s feet, till he allowed\r\nhis eyes to travel upwards over Diggory\xe2\x80\x99s form, as illuminated by the\r\ncandle, to his head and face. \xe2\x80\x9cHa-ha! Well, I suppose I deserve it,\r\nconsidering how I have played with them both,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, as much\r\nto himself as to Venn. \xe2\x80\x9cBut of all the odd things that ever I knew, the\r\noddest is that you should so run counter to your own interests as to\r\nbring this to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy interests?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. \xe2\x80\x99Twas your interest not to do anything which would send me\r\ncourting Thomasin again, now she has accepted you\xe2\x80\x94or something like it.\r\nMrs. Yeobright says you are to marry her. \xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t true, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord! I heard of this before, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe it. When did she\r\nsay so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWildeve began humming as the reddleman had done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe it now,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Venn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRu-um-tum-tum,\xe2\x80\x9d sang Wildeve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Lord\xe2\x80\x94how we can imitate!\xe2\x80\x9d said Venn contemptuously. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have this\r\nout. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go straight to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDiggory withdrew with an emphatic step, Wildeve\xe2\x80\x99s eye passing over his\r\nform in withering derision, as if he were no more than a heath-cropper.\r\nWhen the reddleman\xe2\x80\x99s figure could no longer be seen, Wildeve himself\r\ndescended and plunged into the rayless hollow of the vale.\r\n\r\nTo lose the two women\xe2\x80\x94he who had been the well-beloved of both\xe2\x80\x94was too\r\nironical an issue to be endured. He could only decently save himself by\r\nThomasin; and once he became her husband, Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s repentance, he\r\nthought, would set in for a long and bitter term. It was no wonder that\r\nWildeve, ignorant of the new man at the back of the scene, should have\r\nsupposed Eustacia to be playing a part. To believe that the letter was\r\nnot the result of some momentary pique, to infer that she really gave\r\nhim up to Thomasin, would have required previous knowledge of her\r\ntransfiguration by that man\xe2\x80\x99s influence. Who was to know that she had\r\ngrown generous in the greediness of a new passion, that in coveting one\r\ncousin she was dealing liberally with another, that in her eagerness to\r\nappropriate she gave way?\r\n\r\nFull of this resolve to marry in haste, and wring the heart of the\r\nproud girl, Wildeve went his way.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Diggory Venn had returned to his van, where he stood looking\r\nthoughtfully into the stove. A new vista was opened up to him. But,\r\nhowever promising Mrs. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s views of him might be as a candidate\r\nfor her niece\xe2\x80\x99s hand, one condition was indispensable to the favour of\r\nThomasin herself, and that was a renunciation of his present wild mode\r\nof life. In this he saw little difficulty.\r\n\r\nHe could not afford to wait till the next day before seeing Thomasin\r\nand detailing his plan. He speedily plunged himself into toilet\r\noperations, pulled a suit of cloth clothes from a box, and in about\r\ntwenty minutes stood before the van-lantern as a reddleman in nothing\r\nbut his face, the vermilion shades of which were not to be removed in a\r\nday. Closing the door and fastening it with a padlock, Venn set off\r\ntowards Blooms-End.\r\n\r\nHe had reached the white palings and laid his hand upon the gate when\r\nthe door of the house opened, and quickly closed again. A female form\r\nhad glided in. At the same time a man, who had seemingly been standing\r\nwith the woman in the porch, came forward from the house till he was\r\nface to face with Venn. It was Wildeve again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan alive, you\xe2\x80\x99ve been quick at it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diggory sarcastically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you slow, as you will find,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd,\xe2\x80\x9d lowering his\r\nvoice, \xe2\x80\x9cyou may as well go back again now. I\xe2\x80\x99ve claimed her, and got\r\nher. Good night, reddleman!\xe2\x80\x9d Thereupon Wildeve walked away.\r\n\r\nVenn\xe2\x80\x99s heart sank within him, though it had not risen unduly high. He\r\nstood leaning over the palings in an indecisive mood for nearly a\r\nquarter of an hour. Then he went up the garden path, knocked, and asked\r\nfor Mrs. Yeobright.\r\n\r\nInstead of requesting him to enter she came to the porch. A discourse\r\nwas carried on between them in low measured tones for the space of ten\r\nminutes or more. At the end of the time Mrs. Yeobright went in, and\r\nVenn sadly retraced his steps into the heath. When he had again\r\nregained his van he lit the lantern, and with an apathetic face at once\r\nbegan to pull off his best clothes, till in the course of a few minutes\r\nhe reappeared as the confirmed and irretrievable reddleman that he had\r\nseemed before.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII.\r\nFirmness Is Discovered in a Gentle Heart\r\n\r\n\r\nOn that evening the interior of Blooms-End, though cosy and\r\ncomfortable, had been rather silent. Clym Yeobright was not at home.\r\nSince the Christmas party he had gone on a few days\xe2\x80\x99 visit to a friend\r\nabout ten miles off.\r\n\r\nThe shadowy form seen by Venn to part from Wildeve in the porch, and\r\nquickly withdraw into the house, was Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s. On entering she threw\r\ndown a cloak which had been carelessly wrapped round her, and came\r\nforward to the light, where Mrs. Yeobright sat at her work-table, drawn\r\nup within the settle, so that part of it projected into the\r\nchimney-corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like your going out after dark alone, Tamsin,\xe2\x80\x9d said her aunt\r\nquietly, without looking up from her work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have only been just outside the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Mrs. Yeobright, struck by a change in the tone of\r\nThomasin\xe2\x80\x99s voice, and observing her. Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s cheek was flushed to a\r\npitch far beyond that which it had reached before her troubles, and her\r\neyes glittered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was _he_ who knocked,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought as much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wishes the marriage to be at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed! What\xe2\x80\x94is he anxious?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Yeobright directed a searching look\r\nupon her niece. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy did not Mr. Wildeve come in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did not wish to. You are not friends with him, he says. He would\r\nlike the wedding to be the day after tomorrow, quite privately; at the\r\nchurch of his parish\xe2\x80\x94not at ours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! And what did you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI agreed to it,\xe2\x80\x9d Thomasin answered firmly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am a practical woman\r\nnow. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in hearts at all. I would marry him under any\r\ncircumstances since\xe2\x80\x94since Clym\xe2\x80\x99s letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA letter was lying on Mrs. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s work-basket, and at Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwords her aunt reopened it, and silently read for the tenth time that\r\nday:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the meaning of this silly story that people are circulating\r\nabout Thomasin and Mr. Wildeve? I should call such a scandal\r\nhumiliating if there was the least chance of its being true. How could\r\nsuch a gross falsehood have arisen? It is said that one should go\r\nabroad to hear news of home, and I appear to have done it. Of course I\r\ncontradict the tale everywhere; but it is very vexing, and I wonder how\r\nit could have originated. It is too ridiculous that such a girl as\r\nThomasin could so mortify us as to get jilted on the wedding day. What\r\nhas she done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Yeobright said sadly, putting down the letter. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\r\nthink you can marry him, do so. And since Mr. Wildeve wishes it to be\r\nunceremonious, let it be that too. I can do nothing. It is all in your\r\nown hands now. My power over your welfare came to an end when you left\r\nthis house to go with him to Anglebury.\xe2\x80\x9d She continued, half in\r\nbitterness, \xe2\x80\x9cI may almost ask, why do you consult me in the matter at\r\nall? If you had gone and married him without saying a word to me, I\r\ncould hardly have been angry\xe2\x80\x94simply because, poor girl, you can\xe2\x80\x99t do a\r\nbetter thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say that and dishearten me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right\xe2\x80\x94I will not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not plead for him, Aunt. Human nature is weak, and I am not a\r\nblind woman to insist that he is perfect. I did think so, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnow. But I know my course, and you know that I know it. I hope for the\r\nbest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so do I, and we will both continue to,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright,\r\nrising and kissing her. \xe2\x80\x9cThen the wedding, if it comes off, will be on\r\nthe morning of the very day Clym comes home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I decided that it ought to be over before he came. After that you\r\ncan look him in the face, and so can I. Our concealments will matter\r\nnothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright moved her head in thoughtful assent, and presently said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you wish me to give you away? I am willing to undertake that, you\r\nknow, if you wish, as I was last time. After once forbidding the banns\r\nI think I can do no less.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I will ask you to come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Thomasin reluctantly, but\r\nwith decision. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be unpleasant, I am almost sure. Better let\r\nthere be only strangers present, and none of my relations at all. I\r\nwould rather have it so. I do not wish to do anything which may touch\r\nyour credit, and I feel that I should be uncomfortable if you were\r\nthere, after what has passed. I am only your niece, and there is no\r\nnecessity why you should concern yourself more about me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he has beaten us,\xe2\x80\x9d her aunt said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt really seems as if he had\r\nbeen playing with you in this way in revenge for my humbling him as I\r\ndid by standing up against him at first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, Aunt,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Thomasin.\r\n\r\nThey said no more on the subject then. Diggory Venn\xe2\x80\x99s knock came soon\r\nafter; and Mrs. Yeobright, on returning from her interview with him in\r\nthe porch, carelessly observed, \xe2\x80\x9cAnother lover has come to ask for\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that queer young man Venn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsks to pay his addresses to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and I told him he was too late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThomasin looked silently into the candle-flame. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Diggory!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, and then aroused herself to other things.\r\n\r\nThe next day was passed in mere mechanical deeds of preparation, both\r\nthe women being anxious to immerse themselves in these to escape the\r\nemotional aspect of the situation. Some wearing apparel and other\r\narticles were collected anew for Thomasin, and remarks on domestic\r\ndetails were frequently made, so as to obscure any inner misgivings\r\nabout her future as Wildeve\xe2\x80\x99s wife.\r\n\r\nThe appointed morning came. The arrangement with Wildeve was that he\r\nshould meet her at the church to guard against any unpleasant curiosity\r\nwhich might have affected them had they been seen walking off together\r\nin the usual country way.\r\n\r\nAunt and niece stood together in the bedroom where the bride was\r\ndressing. The sun, where it could catch it, made a mirror of Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhair, which she always wore braided. It was braided according to a\r\ncalendar system\xe2\x80\x94the more important the day the more numerous the\r\nstrands in the braid. On ordinary working-days she braided it in\r\nthrees; on ordinary Sundays in fours; at Maypolings, gipsyings, and the\r\nlike, she braided it in fives. Years ago she had said that when she\r\nmarried she would braid it in sevens. She had braided it in sevens\r\ntoday.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been thinking that I will wear my blue silk after all,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is my wedding day, even though there may be something sad\r\nabout the time. I mean,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, anxious to correct any wrong\r\nimpression, \xe2\x80\x9cnot sad in itself, but in its having had great\r\ndisappointment and trouble before it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright breathed in a way which might have been called a sigh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI almost wish Clym had been at home,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course you chose\r\nthe time because of his absence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPartly. I have felt that I acted unfairly to him in not telling him\r\nall; but, as it was done not to grieve him, I thought I would carry out\r\nthe plan to its end, and tell the whole story when the sky was clear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a practical little woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwish you and he\xe2\x80\x94no, I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish anything. There, it is nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe interrupted, hearing a whizz and a dinging downstairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told Damon I would leave at nine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Thomasin, hastening out of\r\nthe room.\r\n\r\nHer aunt followed. When Thomasin was going up the little walk from the\r\ndoor to the wicket-gate, Mrs. Yeobright looked reluctantly at her, and\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a shame to let you go alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is necessary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Thomasin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d added her aunt with forced cheerfulness, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall call\r\nupon you this afternoon, and bring the cake with me. If Clym has\r\nreturned by that time he will perhaps come too. I wish to show Mr.\r\nWildeve that I bear him no ill-will. Let the past be forgotten. Well,\r\nGod bless you! There, I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in old superstitions, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d She threw a slipper at the retreating figure of the girl, who\r\nturned, smiled, and went on again.\r\n\r\nA few steps further, and she looked back. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you call me, Aunt?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ntremulously inquired. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMoved by an uncontrollable feeling as she looked upon Mrs. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nworn, wet face, she ran back, when her aunt came forward, and they met\r\nagain. \xe2\x80\x9cO\xe2\x80\x94Tamsie,\xe2\x80\x9d said the elder, weeping, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like to let you\r\ngo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I am\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Thomasin began, giving way likewise. But, quelling her grief,\r\nshe said \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d again and went on.\r\n\r\nThen Mrs. Yeobright saw a little figure wending its way between the\r\nscratching furze-bushes, and diminishing far up the valley\xe2\x80\x94a pale-blue\r\nspot in a vast field of neutral brown, solitary and undefended except\r\nby the power of her own hope.\r\n\r\nBut the worst feature in the case was one which did not appear in the\r\nlandscape; it was the man.\r\n\r\nThe hour chosen for the ceremony by Thomasin and Wildeve had been so\r\ntimed as to enable her to escape the awkwardness of meeting her cousin\r\nClym, who was returning the same morning. To own to the partial truth\r\nof what he had heard would be distressing as long as the humiliating\r\nposition resulting from the event was unimproved. It was only after a\r\nsecond and successful journey to the altar that she could lift up her\r\nhead and prove the failure of the first attempt a pure accident.\r\n\r\nShe had not been gone from Blooms-End more than half an hour when\r\nYeobright came by the meads from the other direction and entered the\r\nhouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had an early breakfast,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his mother after greeting her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I could eat a little more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey sat down to the repeated meal, and he went on in a low, anxious\r\nvoice, apparently imagining that Thomasin had not yet come downstairs,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s this I have heard about Thomasin and Mr. Wildeve?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true in many points,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright quietly; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is\r\nall right now, I hope.\xe2\x80\x9d She looked at the clock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThomasin is gone to him today.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym pushed away his breakfast. \xe2\x80\x9cThen there is a scandal of some sort,\r\nand that\xe2\x80\x99s what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with Thomasin. Was it this that made her\r\nill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Not a scandal\xe2\x80\x94a misfortune. I will tell you all about it, Clym.\r\nYou must not be angry, but you must listen, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll find that what\r\nwe have done has been done for the best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe then told him the circumstances. All that he had known of the\r\naffair before he returned from Paris was that there had existed an\r\nattachment between Thomasin and Wildeve, which his mother had at first\r\ndiscountenanced, but had since, owing to the arguments of Thomasin,\r\nlooked upon in a little more favourable light. When she, therefore,\r\nproceeded to explain all he was greatly surprised and troubled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she determined that the wedding should be over before you came\r\nback,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there might be no chance of her\r\nmeeting you, and having a very painful time of it. That\xe2\x80\x99s why she has\r\ngone to him; they have arranged to be married this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I can\xe2\x80\x99t understand it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright, rising. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis so unlike\r\nher. I can see why you did not write to me after her unfortunate return\r\nhome. But why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you let me know when the wedding was going to\r\nbe\xe2\x80\x94the first time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I felt vexed with her just then. She seemed to me to be\r\nobstinate; and when I found that you were nothing in her mind I vowed\r\nthat she should be nothing in yours. I felt that she was only my niece\r\nafter all; I told her she might marry, but that I should take no\r\ninterest in it, and should not bother you about it either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been bothering me. Mother, you did wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought it might disturb you in your business, and that you might\r\nthrow up your situation, or injure your prospects in some way because\r\nof it, so I said nothing. Of course, if they had married at that time\r\nin a proper manner, I should have told you at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTamsin actually being married while we are sitting here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Unless some accident happens again, as it did the first time. It\r\nmay, considering he\xe2\x80\x99s the same man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and I believe it will. Was it right to let her go? Suppose\r\nWildeve is really a bad fellow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he won\xe2\x80\x99t come, and she\xe2\x80\x99ll come home again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should have looked more into it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is useless to say that,\xe2\x80\x9d his mother answered with an impatient look\r\nof sorrow. \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know how bad it has been here with us all these\r\nweeks, Clym. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know what a mortification anything of that sort\r\nis to a woman. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know the sleepless nights we\xe2\x80\x99ve had in this\r\nhouse, and the almost bitter words that have passed between us since\r\nthat Fifth of November. I hope never to pass seven such weeks again.\r\nTamsin has not gone outside the door, and I have been ashamed to look\r\nanybody in the face; and now you blame me for letting her do the only\r\nthing that can be done to set that trouble straight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cUpon the whole I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame you. But just\r\nconsider how sudden it seems to me. Here was I, knowing nothing; and\r\nthen I am told all at once that Tamsie is gone to be married. Well, I\r\nsuppose there was nothing better to do. Do you know, Mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncontinued after a moment or two, looking suddenly interested in his own\r\npast history, \xe2\x80\x9cI once thought of Tamsin as a sweetheart? Yes, I did.\r\nHow odd boys are! And when I came home and saw her this time she seemed\r\nso much more affectionate than usual, that I was quite reminded of\r\nthose days, particularly on the night of the party, when she was\r\nunwell. We had the party just the same\xe2\x80\x94was not that rather cruel to\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt made no difference. I had arranged to give one, and it was not\r\nworth while to make more gloom than necessary. To begin by shutting\r\nourselves up and telling you of Tamsin\xe2\x80\x99s misfortunes would have been a\r\npoor sort of welcome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym remained thinking. \xe2\x80\x9cI almost wish you had not had that party,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid; \xe2\x80\x9cand for other reasons. But I will tell you in a day or two. We\r\nmust think of Tamsin now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey lapsed into silence. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright again,\r\nin a tone which showed some slumbering feeling still. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\r\nkind to Tamsin to let her be married like this, and neither of us there\r\nto keep up her spirits or care a bit about her. She hasn\xe2\x80\x99t disgraced\r\nherself, or done anything to deserve that. It is bad enough that the\r\nwedding should be so hurried and unceremonious, without our keeping\r\naway from it in addition. Upon my soul, \xe2\x80\x99tis almost a shame. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is over by this time,\xe2\x80\x9d said his mother with a sigh; \xe2\x80\x9cunless they\r\nwere late, or he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I shall be soon enough to see them come out. I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite like\r\nyour keeping me in ignorance, Mother, after all. Really, I half hope he\r\nhas failed to meet her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd ruined her character?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense\xe2\x80\x94that wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ruin Thomasin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took up his hat and hastily left the house. Mrs. Yeobright looked\r\nrather unhappy, and sat still, deep in thought. But she was not long\r\nleft alone. A few minutes later Clym came back again, and in his\r\ncompany came Diggory Venn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI find there isn\xe2\x80\x99t time for me to get there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she married?\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Yeobright inquired, turning to the reddleman a\r\nface in which a strange strife of wishes, for and against, was\r\napparent.\r\n\r\nVenn bowed. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow strange it sounds,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he didn\xe2\x80\x99t disappoint her this time?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did not. And there is now no slight on her name. I was hastening\r\nath\xe2\x80\x99art to tell you at once, as I saw you were not there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow came you to be there? How did you know it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been in that neighbourhood for some time, and I saw them go\r\nin,\xe2\x80\x9d said the reddleman. \xe2\x80\x9cWildeve came up to the door, punctual as the\r\nclock. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t expect it of him.\xe2\x80\x9d He did not add, as he might have\r\nadded, that how he came to be in that neighbourhood was not by\r\naccident; that, since Wildeve\xe2\x80\x99s resumption of his right to Thomasin,\r\nVenn, with the thoroughness which was part of his character, had\r\ndetermined to see the end of the episode.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was there?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody hardly. I stood right out of the way, and she did not see me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThe reddleman spoke huskily, and looked into the garden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho gave her away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Vye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow very remarkable! Miss Vye! It is to be considered an honour, I\r\nsuppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s Miss Vye?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Vye\xe2\x80\x99s granddaughter, of Mistover Knap.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA proud girl from Budmouth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright. \xe2\x80\x9cOne not much to my\r\nliking. People say she\xe2\x80\x99s a witch, but of course that\xe2\x80\x99s absurd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe reddleman kept to himself his acquaintance with that fair\r\npersonage, and also that Eustacia was there because he went to fetch\r\nher, in accordance with a promise he had given as soon as he learnt\r\nthat the marriage was to take place. He merely said, in continuation of\r\nthe story\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was sitting on the churchyard wall when they came up, one from one\r\nway, the other from the other; and Miss Vye was walking thereabouts,\r\nlooking at the headstones. As soon as they had gone in I went to the\r\ndoor, feeling I should like to see it, as I knew her so well. I pulled\r\noff my boots because they were so noisy, and went up into the gallery.\r\nI saw then that the parson and clerk were already there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow came Miss Vye to have anything to do with it, if she was only on a\r\nwalk that way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause there was nobody else. She had gone into the church just\r\nbefore me, not into the gallery. The parson looked round before\r\nbeginning, and as she was the only one near he beckoned to her, and she\r\nwent up to the rails. After that, when it came to signing the book, she\r\npushed up her veil and signed; and Tamsin seemed to thank her for her\r\nkindness.\xe2\x80\x9d The reddleman told the tale thoughtfully for there lingered\r\nupon his vision the changing colour of Wildeve, when Eustacia lifted\r\nthe thick veil which had concealed her from recognition and looked\r\ncalmly into his face. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Diggory sadly, \xe2\x80\x9cI came away, for\r\nher history as Tamsin Yeobright was over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI offered to go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright regretfully. \xe2\x80\x9cBut she said it\r\nwas not necessary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is no matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said the reddleman. \xe2\x80\x9cThe thing is done at last\r\nas it was meant to be at first, and God send her happiness. Now I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwish you good morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe placed his cap on his head and went out.\r\n\r\nFrom that instant of leaving Mrs. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s door, the reddleman was\r\nseen no more in or about Egdon Heath for a space of many months. He\r\nvanished entirely. The nook among the brambles where his van had been\r\nstanding was as vacant as ever the next morning, and scarcely a sign\r\nremained to show that he had been there, excepting a few straws, and a\r\nlittle redness on the turf, which was washed away by the next storm of\r\nrain.\r\n\r\nThe report that Diggory had brought of the wedding, correct as far as\r\nit went, was deficient in one significant particular, which had escaped\r\nhim through his being at some distance back in the church. When\r\nThomasin was tremblingly engaged in signing her name Wildeve had flung\r\ntowards Eustacia a glance that said plainly, \xe2\x80\x9cI have punished you now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe had replied in a low tone\xe2\x80\x94and he little thought how truly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nmistake; it gives me sincerest pleasure to see her your wife today.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nBOOK THIRD\xe2\x80\x94THE FASCINATION\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Mind to Me a Kingdom Is\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nIn Clym Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s face could be dimly seen the typical countenance of\r\nthe future. Should there be a classic period to art hereafter, its\r\nPheidias may produce such faces. The view of life as a thing to be put\r\nup with, replacing that zest for existence which was so intense in\r\nearly civilizations, must ultimately enter so thoroughly into the\r\nconstitution of the advanced races that its facial expression will\r\nbecome accepted as a new artistic departure. People already feel that a\r\nman who lives without disturbing a curve of feature, or setting a mark\r\nof mental concern anywhere upon himself, is too far removed from modern\r\nperceptiveness to be a modern type. Physically beautiful men\xe2\x80\x94the glory\r\nof the race when it was young\xe2\x80\x94are almost an anachronism now; and we may\r\nwonder whether, at some time or other, physically beautiful women may\r\nnot be an anachronism likewise.\r\n\r\nThe truth seems to be that a long line of disillusive centuries has\r\npermanently displaced the Hellenic idea of life, or whatever it may be\r\ncalled. What the Greeks only suspected we know well; what their\r\n\xc3\x86schylus imagined our nursery children feel. That old-fashioned\r\nrevelling in the general situation grows less and less possible as we\r\nuncover the defects of natural laws, and see the quandary that man is\r\nin by their operation.\r\n\r\nThe lineaments which will get embodied in ideals based upon this new\r\nrecognition will probably be akin to those of Yeobright. The observer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neye was arrested, not by his face as a picture, but by his face as a\r\npage; not by what it was, but by what it recorded. His features were\r\nattractive in the light of symbols, as sounds intrinsically common\r\nbecome attractive in language, and as shapes intrinsically simple\r\nbecome interesting in writing.\r\n\r\nHe had been a lad of whom something was expected. Beyond this all had\r\nbeen chaos. That he would be successful in an original way, or that he\r\nwould go to the dogs in an original way, seemed equally probable. The\r\nonly absolute certainty about him was that he would not stand still in\r\nthe circumstances amid which he was born.\r\n\r\nHence, when his name was casually mentioned by neighbouring yeomen, the\r\nlistener said, \xe2\x80\x9cAh, Clym Yeobright\xe2\x80\x94what is he doing now?\xe2\x80\x9d When the\r\ninstinctive question about a person is, What is he doing? it is felt\r\nthat he will be found to be, like most of us, doing nothing in\r\nparticular. There is an indefinite sense that he must be invading some\r\nregion of singularity, good or bad. The devout hope is that he is doing\r\nwell. The secret faith is that he is making a mess of it. Half a dozen\r\ncomfortable market-men, who were habitual callers at the Quiet Woman as\r\nthey passed by in their carts, were partial to the topic. In fact,\r\nthough they were not Egdon men, they could hardly avoid it while they\r\nsucked their long clay tubes and regarded the heath through the window.\r\nClym had been so inwoven with the heath in his boyhood that hardly\r\nanybody could look upon it without thinking of him. So the subject\r\nrecurred: if he were making a fortune and a name, so much the better\r\nfor him; if he were making a tragical figure in the world, so much the\r\nbetter for a narrative.\r\n\r\nThe fact was that Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s fame had spread to an awkward extent\r\nbefore he left home. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is bad when your fame outruns your means,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Spanish Jesuit Gracian. At the age of six he had asked a\r\nScripture riddle: \xe2\x80\x9cWho was the first man known to wear breeches?\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\napplause had resounded from the very verge of the heath. At seven he\r\npainted the Battle of Waterloo with tiger-lily pollen and black-currant\r\njuice, in the absence of water-colours. By the time he reached twelve\r\nhe had in this manner been heard of as artist and scholar for at least\r\ntwo miles round. An individual whose fame spreads three or four\r\nthousand yards in the time taken by the fame of others similarly\r\nsituated to travel six or eight hundred, must of necessity have\r\nsomething in him. Possibly Clym\xe2\x80\x99s fame, like Homer\xe2\x80\x99s, owed something to\r\nthe accidents of his situation; nevertheless famous he was.\r\n\r\nHe grew up and was helped out in life. That waggery of fate which\r\nstarted Clive as a writing clerk, Gay as a linen-draper, Keats as a\r\nsurgeon, and a thousand others in a thousand other odd ways, banished\r\nthe wild and ascetic heath lad to a trade whose sole concern was with\r\nthe especial symbols of self-indulgence and vainglory.\r\n\r\nThe details of this choice of a business for him it is not necessary to\r\ngive. At the death of his father a neighbouring gentleman had kindly\r\nundertaken to give the boy a start, and this assumed the form of\r\nsending him to Budmouth. Yeobright did not wish to go there, but it was\r\nthe only feasible opening. Thence he went to London; and thence,\r\nshortly after, to Paris, where he had remained till now.\r\n\r\nSomething being expected of him, he had not been at home many days\r\nbefore a great curiosity as to why he stayed on so long began to arise\r\nin the heath. The natural term of a holiday had passed, yet he still\r\nremained. On the Sunday morning following the week of Thomasin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmarriage a discussion on this subject was in progress at a hair-cutting\r\nbefore Fairway\xe2\x80\x99s house. Here the local barbering was always done at\r\nthis hour on this day, to be followed by the great Sunday wash of the\r\ninhabitants at noon, which in its turn was followed by the great Sunday\r\ndressing an hour later. On Egdon Heath Sunday proper did not begin till\r\ndinner-time, and even then it was a somewhat battered specimen of the\r\nday.\r\n\r\nThese Sunday-morning hair-cuttings were performed by Fairway; the\r\nvictim sitting on a chopping-block in front of the house, without a\r\ncoat, and the neighbours gossiping around, idly observing the locks of\r\nhair as they rose upon the wind after the snip, and flew away out of\r\nsight to the four quarters of the heavens. Summer and winter the scene\r\nwas the same, unless the wind were more than usually blusterous, when\r\nthe stool was shifted a few feet round the corner. To complain of cold\r\nin sitting out of doors, hatless and coatless, while Fairway told true\r\nstories between the cuts of the scissors, would have been to pronounce\r\nyourself no man at once. To flinch, exclaim, or move a muscle of the\r\nface at the small stabs under the ear received from those instruments,\r\nor at scarifications of the neck by the comb, would have been thought a\r\ngross breach of good manners, considering that Fairway did it all for\r\nnothing. A bleeding about the poll on Sunday afternoons was amply\r\naccounted for by the explanation. \xe2\x80\x9cI have had my hair cut, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe conversation on Yeobright had been started by a distant view of the\r\nyoung man rambling leisurely across the heath before them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man who is doing well elsewhere wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t bide here two or three\r\nweeks for nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s got some project in \xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhead\xe2\x80\x94depend upon that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, \xe2\x80\x99a can\xe2\x80\x99t keep a diment shop here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why he should have had them two heavy boxes home if he had\r\nnot been going to bide; and what there is for him to do here the Lord\r\nin heaven knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore many more surmises could be indulged in Yeobright had come near;\r\nand seeing the hair-cutting group he turned aside to join them.\r\nMarching up, and looking critically at their faces for a moment, he\r\nsaid, without introduction, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, folks, let me guess what you have\r\nbeen talking about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, sure, if you will,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, it is a thing I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have dreamed of doing, otherwise,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nFairway in a tone of integrity; \xe2\x80\x9cbut since you have named it, Master\r\nYeobright, I\xe2\x80\x99ll own that we was talking about \xe2\x80\x99ee. We were wondering\r\nwhat could keep you home here mollyhorning about when you have made\r\nsuch a world-wide name for yourself in the nick-nack trade\xe2\x80\x94now, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe truth o\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright with unexpected earnestness. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not\r\nsorry to have the opportunity. I\xe2\x80\x99ve come home because, all things\r\nconsidered, I can be a trifle less useless here than anywhere else. But\r\nI have only lately found this out. When I first got away from home I\r\nthought this place was not worth troubling about. I thought our life\r\nhere was contemptible. To oil your boots instead of blacking them, to\r\ndust your coat with a switch instead of a brush\xe2\x80\x94was there ever anything\r\nmore ridiculous? I said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo \xe2\x80\x99tis; so \xe2\x80\x99tis!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no\xe2\x80\x94you are wrong; it isn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeg your pardon, we thought that was your maning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as my views changed my course became very depressing. I found\r\nthat I was trying to be like people who had hardly anything in common\r\nwith myself. I was endeavouring to put off one sort of life for another\r\nsort of life, which was not better than the life I had known before. It\r\nwas simply different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue; a sight different,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Paris must be a taking place,\xe2\x80\x9d said Humphrey. \xe2\x80\x9cGrand\r\nshop-winders, trumpets, and drums; and here be we out of doors in all\r\nwinds and weathers\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you mistake me,\xe2\x80\x9d pleaded Clym. \xe2\x80\x9cAll this was very depressing. But\r\nnot so depressing as something I next perceived\xe2\x80\x94that my business was\r\nthe idlest, vainest, most effeminate business that ever a man could be\r\nput to. That decided me\xe2\x80\x94I would give it up and try to follow some\r\nrational occupation among the people I knew best, and to whom I could\r\nbe of most use. I have come home; and this is how I mean to carry out\r\nmy plan. I shall keep a school as near to Egdon as possible, so as to\r\nbe able to walk over here and have a night-school in my mother\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\nBut I must study a little at first, to get properly qualified. Now,\r\nneighbours, I must go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Clym resumed his walk across the heath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll never carry it out in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway. \xe2\x80\x9cIn a few weeks\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll learn to see things otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis good-hearted of the young man,\xe2\x80\x9d said another. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, for my part,\r\nI think he had better mind his business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII.\r\nThe New Course Causes Disappointment\r\n\r\n\r\nYeobright loved his kind. He had a conviction that the want of most men\r\nwas knowledge of a sort which brings wisdom rather than affluence. He\r\nwished to raise the class at the expense of individuals rather than\r\nindividuals at the expense of the class. What was more, he was ready at\r\nonce to be the first unit sacrificed.\r\n\r\nIn passing from the bucolic to the intellectual life the intermediate\r\nstages are usually two at least, frequently many more; and one of those\r\nstages is almost sure to be worldly advanced. We can hardly imagine\r\nbucolic placidity quickening to intellectual aims without imagining\r\nsocial aims as the transitional phase. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s local peculiarity\r\nwas that in striving at high thinking he still cleaved to plain\r\nliving\xe2\x80\x94nay, wild and meagre living in many respects, and brotherliness\r\nwith clowns.\r\n\r\nHe was a John the Baptist who took ennoblement rather than repentance\r\nfor his text. Mentally he was in a provincial future, that is, he was\r\nin many points abreast with the central town thinkers of his date. Much\r\nof this development he may have owed to his studious life in Paris,\r\nwhere he had become acquainted with ethical systems popular at the\r\ntime.\r\n\r\nIn consequence of this relatively advanced position, Yeobright might\r\nhave been called unfortunate. The rural world was not ripe for him. A\r\nman should be only partially before his time\xe2\x80\x94to be completely to the\r\nvanward in aspirations is fatal to fame. Had Philip\xe2\x80\x99s warlike son been\r\nintellectually so far ahead as to have attempted civilization without\r\nbloodshed, he would have been twice the godlike hero that he seemed,\r\nbut nobody would have heard of an Alexander.\r\n\r\nIn the interests of renown the forwardness should lie chiefly in the\r\ncapacity to handle things. Successful propagandists have succeeded\r\nbecause the doctrine they bring into form is that which their listeners\r\nhave for some time felt without being able to shape. A man who\r\nadvocates \xc3\xa6sthetic effort and deprecates social effort is only likely\r\nto be understood by a class to which social effort has become a stale\r\nmatter. To argue upon the possibility of culture before luxury to the\r\nbucolic world may be to argue truly, but it is an attempt to disturb a\r\nsequence to which humanity has been long accustomed. Yeobright\r\npreaching to the Egdon eremites that they might rise to a serene\r\ncomprehensiveness without going through the process of enriching\r\nthemselves was not unlike arguing to ancient Chaldeans that in\r\nascending from earth to the pure empyrean it was not necessary to pass\r\nfirst into the intervening heaven of ether.\r\n\r\nWas Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s mind well-proportioned? No. A well proportioned mind is\r\none which shows no particular bias; one of which we may safely say that\r\nit will never cause its owner to be confined as a madman, tortured as a\r\nheretic, or crucified as a blasphemer. Also, on the other hand, that it\r\nwill never cause him to be applauded as a prophet, revered as a priest,\r\nor exalted as a king. Its usual blessings are happiness and mediocrity.\r\nIt produces the poetry of Rogers, the paintings of West, the statecraft\r\nof North, the spiritual guidance of Tomline; enabling its possessors to\r\nfind their way to wealth, to wind up well, to step with dignity off the\r\nstage, to die comfortably in their beds, and to get the decent monument\r\nwhich, in many cases, they deserve. It never would have allowed\r\nYeobright to do such a ridiculous thing as throw up his business to\r\nbenefit his fellow-creatures.\r\n\r\nHe walked along towards home without attending to paths. If anyone knew\r\nthe heath well it was Clym. He was permeated with its scenes, with its\r\nsubstance, and with its odours. He might be said to be its product. His\r\neyes had first opened thereon; with its appearance all the first images\r\nof his memory were mingled, his estimate of life had been coloured by\r\nit: his toys had been the flint knives and arrow-heads which he found\r\nthere, wondering why stones should \xe2\x80\x9cgrow\xe2\x80\x9d to such odd shapes; his\r\nflowers, the purple bells and yellow furze: his animal kingdom, the\r\nsnakes and croppers; his society, its human haunters. Take all the\r\nvarying hates felt by Eustacia Vye towards the heath, and translate\r\nthem into loves, and you have the heart of Clym. He gazed upon the wide\r\nprospect as he walked, and was glad.\r\n\r\nTo many persons this Egdon was a place which had slipped out of its\r\ncentury generations ago, to intrude as an uncouth object into this. It\r\nwas an obsolete thing, and few cared to study it. How could this be\r\notherwise in the days of square fields, plashed hedges, and meadows\r\nwatered on a plan so rectangular that on a fine day they looked like\r\nsilver gridirons? The farmer, in his ride, who could smile at\r\nartificial grasses, look with solicitude at the coming corn, and sigh\r\nwith sadness at the fly-eaten turnips, bestowed upon the distant upland\r\nof heath nothing better than a frown. But as for Yeobright, when he\r\nlooked from the heights on his way he could not help indulging in a\r\nbarbarous satisfaction at observing that, in some of the attempts at\r\nreclamation from the waste, tillage, after holding on for a year or\r\ntwo, had receded again in despair, the ferns and furze-tufts stubbornly\r\nreasserting themselves.\r\n\r\nHe descended into the valley, and soon reached his home at Blooms-End.\r\nHis mother was snipping dead leaves from the window-plants. She looked\r\nup at him as if she did not understand the meaning of his long stay\r\nwith her; her face had worn that look for several days. He could\r\nperceive that the curiosity which had been shown by the hair-cutting\r\ngroup amounted in his mother to concern. But she had asked no question\r\nwith her lips, even when the arrival of his trunk suggested that he was\r\nnot going to leave her soon. Her silence besought an explanation of him\r\nmore loudly than words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not going back to Paris again, Mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least, in my\r\nold capacity. I have given up the business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright turned in pained surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought something was\r\namiss, because of the boxes. I wonder you did not tell me sooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have done it. But I have been in doubt whether you would be\r\npleased with my plan. I was not quite clear on a few points myself. I\r\nam going to take an entirely new course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am astonished, Clym. How can you want to do better than you\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\ndoing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery easily. But I shall not do better in the way you mean; I suppose\r\nit will be called doing worse. But I hate that business of mine, and I\r\nwant to do some worthy thing before I die. As a schoolmaster I think to\r\ndo it\xe2\x80\x94a school-master to the poor and ignorant, to teach them what\r\nnobody else will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter all the trouble that has been taken to give you a start, and\r\nwhen there is nothing to do but to keep straight on towards affluence,\r\nyou say you will be a poor man\xe2\x80\x99s schoolmaster. Your fancies will be\r\nyour ruin, Clym.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright spoke calmly, but the force of feeling behind the words\r\nwas but too apparent to one who knew her as well as her son did. He did\r\nnot answer. There was in his face that hopelessness of being understood\r\nwhich comes when the objector is constitutionally beyond the reach of a\r\nlogic that, even under favouring conditions, is almost too coarse a\r\nvehicle for the subtlety of the argument.\r\n\r\nNo more was said on the subject till the end of dinner. His mother then\r\nbegan, as if there had been no interval since the morning. \xe2\x80\x9cIt disturbs\r\nme, Clym, to find that you have come home with such thoughts as those.\r\nI hadn\xe2\x80\x99t the least idea that you meant to go backward in the world by\r\nyour own free choice. Of course, I have always supposed you were going\r\nto push straight on, as other men do\xe2\x80\x94all who deserve the name\xe2\x80\x94when they\r\nhave been put in a good way of doing well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym, in a troubled tone. \xe2\x80\x9cMother, I hate the\r\nflashy business. Talk about men who deserve the name, can any man\r\ndeserving the name waste his time in that effeminate way, when he sees\r\nhalf the world going to ruin for want of somebody to buckle to and\r\nteach them how to breast the misery they are born to? I get up every\r\nmorning and see the whole creation groaning and travailing in pain, as\r\nSt. Paul says, and yet there am I, trafficking in glittering splendours\r\nwith wealthy women and titled libertines, and pandering to the meanest\r\nvanities\xe2\x80\x94I, who have health and strength enough for anything. I have\r\nbeen troubled in my mind about it all the year, and the end is that I\r\ncannot do it any more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy can\xe2\x80\x99t you do it as well as others?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, except that there are many things other people care for\r\nwhich I don\xe2\x80\x99t; and that\xe2\x80\x99s partly why I think I ought to do this. For\r\none thing, my body does not require much of me. I cannot enjoy\r\ndelicacies; good things are wasted upon me. Well, I ought to turn that\r\ndefect to advantage, and by being able to do without what other people\r\nrequire I can spend what such things cost upon anybody else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow, Yeobright, having inherited some of these very instincts from the\r\nwoman before him, could not fail to awaken a reciprocity in her through\r\nher feelings, if not by arguments, disguise it as she might for his\r\ngood. She spoke with less assurance. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet you might have been a\r\nwealthy man if you had only persevered. Manager to that large diamond\r\nestablishment\xe2\x80\x94what better can a man wish for? What a post of trust and\r\nrespect! I suppose you will be like your father; like him, you are\r\ngetting weary of doing well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said her son, \xe2\x80\x9cI am not weary of that, though I am weary of what\r\nyou mean by it. Mother, what is doing well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright was far too thoughtful a woman to be content with ready\r\ndefinitions, and, like the \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is wisdom?\xe2\x80\x9d of Plato\xe2\x80\x99s Socrates, and\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is truth?\xe2\x80\x9d of Pontius Pilate, Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s burning question\r\nreceived no answer.\r\n\r\nThe silence was broken by the clash of the garden gate, a tap at the\r\ndoor, and its opening. Christian Cantle appeared in the room in his\r\nSunday clothes.\r\n\r\nIt was the custom on Egdon to begin the preface to a story before\r\nabsolutely entering the house, so as to be well in for the body of the\r\nnarrative by the time visitor and visited stood face to face. Christian\r\nhad been saying to them while the door was leaving its latch, \xe2\x80\x9cTo think\r\nthat I, who go from home but once in a while, and hardly then, should\r\nhave been there this morning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis news you have brought us, then, Christian?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, sure, about a witch, and ye must overlook my time o\xe2\x80\x99 day; for,\r\nsays I, \xe2\x80\x98I must go and tell \xe2\x80\x99em, though they won\xe2\x80\x99t have half done\r\ndinner.\xe2\x80\x99 I assure ye it made me shake like a driven leaf. Do ye think\r\nany harm will come o\xe2\x80\x99t?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis morning at church we was all standing up, and the pa\xe2\x80\x99son said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Let us pray.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 thinks I, \xe2\x80\x98one may as well kneel as stand\xe2\x80\x99; so\r\ndown I went; and, more than that, all the rest were as willing to\r\noblige the man as I. We hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been hard at it for more than a minute\r\nwhen a most terrible screech sounded through church, as if somebody had\r\njust gied up their heart\xe2\x80\x99s blood. All the folk jumped up and then we\r\nfound that Susan Nunsuch had pricked Miss Vye with a long\r\nstocking-needle, as she had threatened to do as soon as ever she could\r\nget the young lady to church, where she don\xe2\x80\x99t come very often. She\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nwaited for this chance for weeks, so as to draw her blood and put an\r\nend to the bewitching of Susan\xe2\x80\x99s children that has been carried on so\r\nlong. Sue followed her into church, sat next to her, and as soon as she\r\ncould find a chance in went the stocking-needle into my lady\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heaven, how horrid!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Yeobright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSue pricked her that deep that the maid fainted away; and as I was\r\nafeard there might be some tumult among us, I got behind the bass viol\r\nand didn\xe2\x80\x99t see no more. But they carried her out into the air, \xe2\x80\x99tis\r\nsaid; but when they looked round for Sue she was gone. What a scream\r\nthat girl gied, poor thing! There were the pa\xe2\x80\x99son in his surplice\r\nholding up his hand and saying, \xe2\x80\x98Sit down, my good people, sit down!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nBut the deuce a bit would they sit down. O, and what d\xe2\x80\x99ye think I found\r\nout, Mrs. Yeobright? The pa\xe2\x80\x99son wears a suit of clothes under his\r\nsurplice!\xe2\x80\x94I could see his black sleeves when he held up his arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a cruel thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said his mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe nation ought to look into it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christian. \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s Humphrey\r\ncoming, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn came Humphrey. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, have ye heard the news? But I see you have.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Tis a very strange thing that whenever one of Egdon folk goes to\r\nchurch some rum job or other is sure to be doing. The last time one of\r\nus was there was when neighbour Fairway went in the fall; and that was\r\nthe day you forbad the banns, Mrs. Yeobright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas this cruelly treated girl been able to walk home?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey say she got better, and went home very well. And now I\xe2\x80\x99ve told it\r\nI must be moving homeward myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Humphrey. \xe2\x80\x9cTruly now we shall see if there\xe2\x80\x99s anything in\r\nwhat folks say about her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they were gone into the heath again Yeobright said quietly to his\r\nmother, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think I have turned teacher too soon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is right that there should be schoolmasters, and missionaries, and\r\nall such men,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is right, too, that I should try to\r\nlift you out of this life into something richer, and that you should\r\nnot come back again, and be as if I had not tried at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLater in the day Sam, the turf-cutter, entered. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve come a-borrowing,\r\nMrs. Yeobright. I suppose you have heard what\xe2\x80\x99s been happening to the\r\nbeauty on the hill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Sam: half a dozen have been telling us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeauty?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, tolerably well-favoured,\xe2\x80\x9d Sam replied. \xe2\x80\x9cLord! all the country\r\nowns that \xe2\x80\x99tis one of the strangest things in the world that such a\r\nwoman should have come to live up there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDark or fair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, though I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen her twenty times, that\xe2\x80\x99s a thing I cannot call\r\nto mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDarker than Tamsin,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mrs. Yeobright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman who seems to care for nothing at all, as you may say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is melancholy, then?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe mopes about by herself, and don\xe2\x80\x99t mix in with the people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she a young lady inclined for adventures?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to my knowledge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t join in with the lads in their games, to get some sort of\r\nexcitement in this lonely place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMumming, for instance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Her notions be different. I should rather say her thoughts were\r\nfar away from here, with lords and ladies she\xe2\x80\x99ll never know, and\r\nmansions she\xe2\x80\x99ll never see again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nObserving that Clym appeared singularly interested Mrs. Yeobright said\r\nrather uneasily to Sam, \xe2\x80\x9cYou see more in her than most of us do. Miss\r\nVye is to my mind too idle to be charming. I have never heard that she\r\nis of any use to herself or to other people. Good girls don\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\ntreated as witches even on Egdon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense\xe2\x80\x94that proves nothing either way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, of course I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand such niceties,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam,\r\nwithdrawing from a possibly unpleasant argument; \xe2\x80\x9cand what she is we\r\nmust wait for time to tell us. The business that I have really called\r\nabout is this, to borrow the longest and strongest rope you have. The\r\ncaptain\xe2\x80\x99s bucket has dropped into the well, and they are in want of\r\nwater; and as all the chaps are at home today we think we can get it\r\nout for him. We have three cart-ropes already, but they won\xe2\x80\x99t reach to\r\nthe bottom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright told him that he might have whatever ropes he could find\r\nin the outhouse, and Sam went out to search. When he passed by the door\r\nClym joined him, and accompanied him to the gate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this young witch-lady going to stay long at Mistover?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a cruel shame to ill-use her, She must have suffered greatly\xe2\x80\x94more\r\nin mind than in body.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas a graceless trick\xe2\x80\x94such a handsome girl, too. You ought to see\r\nher, Mr. Yeobright, being a young man come from far, and with a little\r\nmore to show for your years than most of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think she would like to teach children?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym.\r\n\r\nSam shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cQuite a different sort of body from that, I\r\nreckon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, it was merely something which occurred to me. It would of course be\r\nnecessary to see her and talk it over\xe2\x80\x94not an easy thing, by the way,\r\nfor my family and hers are not very friendly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you how you mid see her, Mr. Yeobright,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are\r\ngoing to grapple for the bucket at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock tonight at her house,\r\nand you could lend a hand. There\xe2\x80\x99s five or six coming, but the well is\r\ndeep, and another might be useful, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind appearing in that\r\nshape. She\xe2\x80\x99s sure to be walking round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll think of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright; and they parted.\r\n\r\nHe thought of it a good deal; but nothing more was said about Eustacia\r\ninside the house at that time. Whether this romantic martyr to\r\nsuperstition and the melancholy mummer he had conversed with under the\r\nfull moon were one and the same person remained as yet a problem.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII.\r\nThe First Act in a Timeworn Drama\r\n\r\n\r\nThe afternoon was fine, and Yeobright walked on the heath for an hour\r\nwith his mother. When they reached the lofty ridge which divided the\r\nvalley of Blooms-End from the adjoining valley they stood still and\r\nlooked round. The Quiet Woman Inn was visible on the low margin of the\r\nheath in one direction, and afar on the other hand rose Mistover Knap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean to call on Thomasin?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. But you need not come this time,\xe2\x80\x9d said his mother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case I\xe2\x80\x99ll branch off here, Mother. I am going to Mistover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright turned to him inquiringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to help them get the bucket out of the captain\xe2\x80\x99s well,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncontinued. \xe2\x80\x9cAs it is so very deep I may be useful. And I should like to\r\nsee this Miss Vye\xe2\x80\x94not so much for her good looks as for another\r\nreason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust you go?\xe2\x80\x9d his mother asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd they parted. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no help for it,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Clym\xe2\x80\x99s mother\r\ngloomily as he withdrew. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are sure to see each other. I wish Sam\r\nwould carry his news to other houses than mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym\xe2\x80\x99s retreating figure got smaller and smaller as it rose and fell\r\nover the hillocks on his way. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is tender-hearted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs.\r\nYeobright to herself while she watched him; \xe2\x80\x9cotherwise it would matter\r\nlittle. How he\xe2\x80\x99s going on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was, indeed, walking with a will over the furze, as straight as a\r\nline, as if his life depended upon it. His mother drew a long breath,\r\nand, abandoning the visit to Thomasin, turned back.
