Her eyes were fixed on the ground; within her two sights were\r\ngraven\xe2\x80\x94that of Clym\xe2\x80\x99s hook and brambles at the door, and that of a\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s face at a window. Her lips trembled, becoming unnaturally thin\r\nas she murmured, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis too much\xe2\x80\x94Clym, how can he bear to do it! He is\r\nat home; and yet he lets her shut the door against me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn her anxiety to get out of the direct view of the house she had\r\ndiverged from the straightest path homeward, and while looking about to\r\nregain it she came upon a little boy gathering whortleberries in a\r\nhollow. The boy was Johnny Nunsuch, who had been Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s stoker at\r\nthe bonfire, and, with the tendency of a minute body to gravitate\r\ntowards a greater, he began hovering round Mrs. Yeobright as soon as\r\nshe appeared, and trotted on beside her without perceptible\r\nconsciousness of his act.\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright spoke to him as one in a mesmeric sleep. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a long\r\nway home, my child, and we shall not get there till evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall,\xe2\x80\x9d said her small companion. \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to play marnels afore\r\nsupper, and we go to supper at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock, because Father comes home.\r\nDoes your father come home at six too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he never comes; nor my son either, nor anybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have made you so down? Have you seen a ooser?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen what\xe2\x80\x99s worse\xe2\x80\x94a woman\xe2\x80\x99s face looking at me through a\r\nwindowpane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that a bad sight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It is always a bad sight to see a woman looking out at a weary\r\nwayfarer and not letting her in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce when I went to Throope Great Pond to catch effets I seed myself\r\nlooking up at myself, and I was frightened and jumped back like\r\nanything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n...\xe2\x80\x9cIf they had only shown signs of meeting my advances halfway how\r\nwell it might have been done! But there is no chance. Shut out! She\r\nmust have set him against me. Can there be beautiful bodies without\r\nhearts inside? I think so. I would not have done it against a\r\nneighbour\xe2\x80\x99s cat on such a fiery day as this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever again\xe2\x80\x94never! Not even if they send for me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must be a very curious woman to talk like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no, not at all,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, returning to the boy\xe2\x80\x99s prattle. \xe2\x80\x9cMost\r\npeople who grow up and have children talk as I do. When you grow up\r\nyour mother will talk as I do too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope she won\xe2\x80\x99t; because \xe2\x80\x99tis very bad to talk nonsense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, child; it is nonsense, I suppose. Are you not nearly spent with\r\nthe heat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. But not so much as you be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour face is white and wet, and your head is hanging-down-like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I am exhausted from inside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you, every time you take a step, go like this?\xe2\x80\x9d The child in\r\nspeaking gave to his motion the jerk and limp of an invalid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I have a burden which is more than I can bear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little boy remained silently pondering, and they tottered on side\r\nby side until more than a quarter of an hour had elapsed, when Mrs.\r\nYeobright, whose weakness plainly increased, said to him, \xe2\x80\x9cI must sit\r\ndown here to rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen she had seated herself he looked long in her face and said, \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nfunny you draw your breath\xe2\x80\x94like a lamb when you drive him till he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnearly done for. Do you always draw your breath like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot always.\xe2\x80\x9d Her voice was now so low as to be scarcely above a\r\nwhisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will go to sleep there, I suppose, won\xe2\x80\x99t you? You have shut your\r\neyes already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I shall not sleep much till\xe2\x80\x94another day, and then I hope to have a\r\nlong, long one\xe2\x80\x94very long. Now can you tell me if Rimsmoor Pond is dry\r\nthis summer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRimsmoor Pond is, but Oker\xe2\x80\x99s Pool isn\xe2\x80\x99t, because he is deep, and is\r\nnever dry\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis just over there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the water clear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, middling\xe2\x80\x94except where the heath-croppers walk into it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, take this, and go as fast as you can, and dip me up the clearest\r\nyou can find. I am very faint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe drew from the small willow reticule that she carried in her hand an\r\nold-fashioned china teacup without a handle; it was one of half a dozen\r\nof the same sort lying in the reticule, which she had preserved ever\r\nsince her childhood, and had brought with her today as a small present\r\nfor Clym and Eustacia.\r\n\r\nThe boy started on his errand, and soon came back with the water, such\r\nas it was. Mrs. Yeobright attempted to drink, but it was so warm as to\r\ngive her nausea, and she threw it away. Afterwards she still remained\r\nsitting, with her eyes closed.\r\n\r\nThe boy waited, played near her, caught several of the little brown\r\nbutterflies which abounded, and then said as he waited again, \xe2\x80\x9cI like\r\ngoing on better than biding still. Will you soon start again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I might go on by myself,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed, fearing, apparently, that\r\nhe was to be pressed into some unpleasant service. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you want me any\r\nmore, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright made no reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I tell Mother?\xe2\x80\x9d the boy continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell her you have seen a broken-hearted woman cast off by her son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore quite leaving her he threw upon her face a wistful glance, as if\r\nhe had misgivings on the generosity of forsaking her thus. He gazed\r\ninto her face in a vague, wondering manner, like that of one examining\r\nsome strange old manuscript the key to whose characters is\r\nundiscoverable. He was not so young as to be absolutely without a sense\r\nthat sympathy was demanded, he was not old enough to be free from the\r\nterror felt in childhood at beholding misery in adult quarters hitherto\r\ndeemed impregnable; and whether she were in a position to cause trouble\r\nor to suffer from it, whether she and her affliction were something to\r\npity or something to fear, it was beyond him to decide. He lowered his\r\neyes and went on without another word. Before he had gone half a mile\r\nhe had forgotten all about her, except that she was a woman who had sat\r\ndown to rest.\r\n\r\nMrs. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s exertions, physical and emotional, had well-nigh\r\nprostrated her; but she continued to creep along in short stages with\r\nlong breaks between. The sun had now got far to the west of south and\r\nstood directly in her face, like some merciless incendiary, brand in\r\nhand, waiting to consume her. With the departure of the boy all visible\r\nanimation disappeared from the landscape, though the intermittent husky\r\nnotes of the male grasshoppers from every tuft of furze were enough to\r\nshow that amid the prostration of the larger animal species an unseen\r\ninsect world was busy in all the fullness of life.\r\n\r\nIn two hours she reached a slope about three-fourths the whole distance\r\nfrom Alderworth to her own home, where a little patch of\r\nshepherd\xe2\x80\x99s-thyme intruded upon the path; and she sat down upon the\r\nperfumed mat it formed there. In front of her a colony of ants had\r\nestablished a thoroughfare across the way, where they toiled a\r\nnever-ending and heavy-laden throng. To look down upon them was like\r\nobserving a city street from the top of a tower. She remembered that\r\nthis bustle of ants had been in progress for years at the same\r\nspot\xe2\x80\x94doubtless those of the old times were the ancestors of these which\r\nwalked there now. She leant back to obtain more thorough rest, and the\r\nsoft eastern portion of the sky was as great a relief to her eyes as\r\nthe thyme was to her head. While she looked a heron arose on that side\r\nof the sky and flew on with his face towards the sun. He had come\r\ndripping wet from some pool in the valleys, and as he flew the edges\r\nand lining of his wings, his thighs and his breast were so caught by\r\nthe bright sunbeams that he appeared as if formed of burnished silver.\r\nUp in the zenith where he was seemed a free and happy place, away from\r\nall contact with the earthly ball to which she was pinioned; and she\r\nwished that she could arise uncrushed from its surface and fly as he\r\nflew then.\r\n\r\nBut, being a mother, it was inevitable that she should soon cease to\r\nruminate upon her own condition. Had the track of her next thought been\r\nmarked by a streak in the air, like the path of a meteor, it would have\r\nshown a direction contrary to the heron\xe2\x80\x99s, and have descended to the\r\neastward upon the roof of Clym\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVII.\r\nThe Tragic Meeting of Two Old Friends\r\n\r\n\r\nHe in the meantime had aroused himself from sleep, sat up, and looked\r\naround. Eustacia was sitting in a chair hard by him, and though she\r\nheld a book in her hand she had not looked into it for some time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym, brushing his eyes with his hands. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\nsoundly I have slept! I have had such a tremendous dream, too\xe2\x80\x94one I\r\nshall never forget.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you had been dreaming,\xe2\x80\x9d said she.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. It was about my mother. I dreamt that I took you to her house to\r\nmake up differences, and when we got there we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get in, though\r\nshe kept on crying to us for help. However, dreams are dreams. What\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock is it, Eustacia?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf-past two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo late, is it? I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to stay so long. By the time I have had\r\nsomething to eat it will be after three.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnn is not come back from the village, and I thought I would let you\r\nsleep on till she returned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym went to the window and looked out. Presently he said, musingly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWeek after week passes, and yet Mother does not come. I thought I\r\nshould have heard something from her long before this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMisgiving, regret, fear, resolution, ran their swift course of\r\nexpression in Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s dark eyes. She was face to face with a\r\nmonstrous difficulty, and she resolved to get free of it by\r\npostponement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must certainly go to Blooms-End soon,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cand I think I\r\nhad better go alone.\xe2\x80\x9d He picked up his leggings and gloves, threw them\r\ndown again, and added, \xe2\x80\x9cAs dinner will be so late today I will not go\r\nback to the heath, but work in the garden till the evening, and then,\r\nwhen it will be cooler, I will walk to Blooms-End. I am quite sure that\r\nif I make a little advance Mother will be willing to forget all. It\r\nwill be rather late before I can get home, as I shall not be able to do\r\nthe distance either way in less than an hour and a half. But you will\r\nnot mind for one evening, dear? What are you thinking of to make you\r\nlook so abstracted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said heavily. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish we didn\xe2\x80\x99t live here,\r\nClym. The world seems all wrong in this place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94if we make it so. I wonder if Thomasin has been to Blooms-End\r\nlately. I hope so. But probably not, as she is, I believe, expecting to\r\nbe confined in a month or so. I wish I had thought of that before. Poor\r\nMother must indeed be very lonely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like you going tonight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not tonight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething may be said which will terribly injure me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother is not vindictive,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym, his colour faintly rising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I wish you would not go,\xe2\x80\x9d Eustacia repeated in a low tone. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\r\nagree not to go tonight I promise to go by myself to her house\r\ntomorrow, and make it up with her, and wait till you fetch me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you want to do that at this particular time, when at every\r\nprevious time that I have proposed it you have refused?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot explain further than that I should like to see her alone\r\nbefore you go,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, with an impatient move of her head, and\r\nlooking at him with an anxiety more frequently seen upon those of a\r\nsanguine temperament than upon such as herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is very odd that just when I had decided to go myself you\r\nshould want to do what I proposed long ago. If I wait for you to go\r\ntomorrow another day will be lost; and I know I shall be unable to rest\r\nanother night without having been. I want to get this settled, and\r\nwill. You must visit her afterwards\xe2\x80\x94it will be all the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI could even go with you now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou could scarcely walk there and back without a longer rest than I\r\nshall take. No, not tonight, Eustacia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet it be as you say, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied in the quiet way of one who,\r\nthough willing to ward off evil consequences by a mild effort, would\r\nlet events fall out as they might sooner than wrestle hard to direct\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nClym then went into the garden; and a thoughtful languor stole over\r\nEustacia for the remainder of the afternoon, which her husband\r\nattributed to the heat of the weather.\r\n\r\nIn the evening he set out on the journey. Although the heat of summer\r\nwas yet intense the days had considerably shortened, and before he had\r\nadvanced a mile on his way all the heath purples, browns, and greens\r\nhad merged in a uniform dress without airiness or graduation, and\r\nbroken only by touches of white where the little heaps of clean quartz\r\nsand showed the entrance to a rabbit burrow, or where the white flints\r\nof a footpath lay like a thread over the slopes. In almost every one of\r\nthe isolated and stunted thorns which grew here and there a nighthawk\r\nrevealed his presence by whirring like the clack of a mill as long as\r\nhe could hold his breath, then stopping, flapping his wings, wheeling\r\nround the bush, alighting, and after a silent interval of listening\r\nbeginning to whirr again. At each brushing of Clym\xe2\x80\x99s feet white\r\nmillermoths flew into the air just high enough to catch upon their\r\ndusty wings the mellowed light from the west, which now shone across\r\nthe depressions and levels of the ground without falling thereon to\r\nlight them up.\r\n\r\nYeobright walked on amid this quiet scene with a hope that all would\r\nsoon be well. Three miles on he came to a spot where a soft perfume was\r\nwafted across his path, and he stood still for a moment to inhale the\r\nfamiliar scent. It was the place at which, four hours earlier, his\r\nmother had sat down exhausted on the knoll covered with\r\nshepherd\xe2\x80\x99s-thyme. While he stood a sound between a breathing and a moan\r\nsuddenly reached his ears.\r\n\r\nHe looked to where the sound came from; but nothing appeared there save\r\nthe verge of the hillock stretching against the sky in an unbroken\r\nline. He moved a few steps in that direction, and now he perceived a\r\nrecumbent figure almost close to his feet.\r\n\r\nAmong the different possibilities as to the person\xe2\x80\x99s individuality\r\nthere did not for a moment occur to Yeobright that it might be one of\r\nhis own family. Sometimes furze-cutters had been known to sleep out of\r\ndoors at these times, to save a long journey homeward and back again;\r\nbut Clym remembered the moan and looked closer, and saw that the form\r\nwas feminine; and a distress came over him like cold air from a cave.\r\nBut he was not absolutely certain that the woman was his mother till he\r\nstooped and beheld her face, pallid, and with closed eyes.\r\n\r\nHis breath went, as it were, out of his body and the cry of anguish\r\nwhich would have escaped him died upon his lips. During the momentary\r\ninterval that elapsed before he became conscious that something must be\r\ndone all sense of time and place left him, and it seemed as if he and\r\nhis mother were as when he was a child with her many years ago on this\r\nheath at hours similar to the present. Then he awoke to activity; and\r\nbending yet lower he found that she still breathed, and that her breath\r\nthough feeble was regular, except when disturbed by an occasional gasp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, what is it! Mother, are you very ill\xe2\x80\x94you are not dying?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried,\r\npressing his lips to her face. \xe2\x80\x9cI am your Clym. How did you come here?\r\nWhat does it all mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment the chasm in their lives which his love for Eustacia had\r\ncaused was not remembered by Yeobright, and to him the present joined\r\ncontinuously with that friendly past that had been their experience\r\nbefore the division.\r\n\r\nShe moved her lips, appeared to know him, but could not speak; and then\r\nClym strove to consider how best to move her, as it would be necessary\r\nto get her away from the spot before the dews were intense. He was\r\nable-bodied, and his mother was thin. He clasped his arms round her,\r\nlifted her a little, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cDoes that hurt you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook her head, and he lifted her up; then, at a slow pace, went\r\nonward with his load. The air was now completely cool; but whenever he\r\npassed over a sandy patch of ground uncarpeted with vegetation there\r\nwas reflected from its surface into his face the heat which it had\r\nimbibed during the day. At the beginning of his undertaking he had\r\nthought but little of the distance which yet would have to be traversed\r\nbefore Blooms-End could be reached; but though he had slept that\r\nafternoon he soon began to feel the weight of his burden. Thus he\r\nproceeded, like \xc3\x86neas with his father; the bats circling round his\r\nhead, nightjars flapping their wings within a yard of his face, and not\r\na human being within call.\r\n\r\nWhile he was yet nearly a mile from the house his mother exhibited\r\nsigns of restlessness under the constraint of being borne along, as if\r\nhis arms were irksome to her. He lowered her upon his knees and looked\r\naround. The point they had now reached, though far from any road, was\r\nnot more than a mile from the Blooms-End cottages occupied by Fairway,\r\nSam, Humphrey, and the Cantles. Moreover, fifty yards off stood a hut,\r\nbuilt of clods and covered with thin turves, but now entirely disused.\r\nThe simple outline of the lonely shed was visible, and thither he\r\ndetermined to direct his steps. As soon as he arrived he laid her down\r\ncarefully by the entrance, and then ran and cut with his pocketknife an\r\narmful of the dryest fern. Spreading this within the shed, which was\r\nentirely open on one side, he placed his mother thereon; then he ran\r\nwith all his might towards the dwelling of Fairway.\r\n\r\nNearly a quarter of an hour had passed, disturbed only by the broken\r\nbreathing of the sufferer, when moving figures began to animate the\r\nline between heath and sky. In a few moments Clym arrived with Fairway,\r\nHumphrey, and Susan Nunsuch; Olly Dowden, who had chanced to be at\r\nFairway\xe2\x80\x99s, Christian and Grandfer Cantle following helter-skelter\r\nbehind. They had brought a lantern and matches, water, a pillow, and a\r\nfew other articles which had occurred to their minds in the hurry of\r\nthe moment. Sam had been despatched back again for brandy, and a boy\r\nbrought Fairway\xe2\x80\x99s pony, upon which he rode off to the nearest medical\r\nman, with directions to call at Wildeve\xe2\x80\x99s on his way, and inform\r\nThomasin that her aunt was unwell.\r\n\r\nSam and the brandy soon arrived, and it was administered by the light\r\nof the lantern; after which she became sufficiently conscious to\r\nsignify by signs that something was wrong with her foot. Olly Dowden at\r\nlength understood her meaning, and examined the foot indicated. It was\r\nswollen and red. Even as they watched the red began to assume a more\r\nlivid colour, in the midst of which appeared a scarlet speck, smaller\r\nthan a pea, and it was found to consist of a drop of blood, which rose\r\nabove the smooth flesh of her ankle in a hemisphere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what it is,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sam. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has been stung by an adder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym instantly. \xe2\x80\x9cI remember when I was a child seeing just\r\nsuch a bite. O, my poor mother!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was my father who was bit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s only one way to\r\ncure it. You must rub the place with the fat of other adders, and the\r\nonly way to get that is by frying them. That\xe2\x80\x99s what they did for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis an old remedy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym distrustfully, \xe2\x80\x9cand I have doubts about\r\nit. But we can do nothing else till the doctor comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a sure cure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Olly Dowden, with emphasis. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve used it when\r\nI used to go out nursing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we must pray for daylight, to catch them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym gloomily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will see what I can do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam.\r\n\r\nHe took a green hazel which he had used as a walking stick, split it at\r\nthe end, inserted a small pebble, and with the lantern in his hand went\r\nout into the heath. Clym had by this time lit a small fire, and\r\ndespatched Susan Nunsuch for a frying pan. Before she had returned Sam\r\ncame in with three adders, one briskly coiling and uncoiling in the\r\ncleft of the stick, and the other two hanging dead across it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have only been able to get one alive and fresh as he ought to be,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Sam. \xe2\x80\x9cThese limp ones are two I killed today at work; but as they\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t die till the sun goes down they can\xe2\x80\x99t be very stale meat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe live adder regarded the assembled group with a sinister look in its\r\nsmall black eye, and the beautiful brown and jet pattern on its back\r\nseemed to intensify with indignation. Mrs. Yeobright saw the creature,\r\nand the creature saw her\xe2\x80\x94she quivered throughout, and averted her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at that,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Christian Cantle. \xe2\x80\x9cNeighbours, how do we know\r\nbut that something of the old serpent in God\xe2\x80\x99s garden, that gied the\r\napple to the young woman with no clothes, lives on in adders and snakes\r\nstill? Look at his eye\xe2\x80\x94for all the world like a villainous sort of\r\nblack currant. \xe2\x80\x99Tis to be hoped he can\xe2\x80\x99t ill-wish us! There\xe2\x80\x99s folks in\r\nheath who\xe2\x80\x99ve been overlooked already. I will never kill another adder\r\nas long as I live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, \xe2\x80\x99tis right to be afeard of things, if folks can\xe2\x80\x99t help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nGrandfer Cantle. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twould have saved me many a brave danger in my\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy I heard something outside the shed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christian. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish\r\ntroubles would come in the daytime, for then a man could show his\r\ncourage, and hardly beg for mercy of the most broomstick old woman he\r\nshould see, if he was a brave man, and able to run out of her sight!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven such an ignorant fellow as I should know better than do that,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Sam.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there\xe2\x80\x99s calamities where we least expect it, whether or no.\r\nNeighbours, if Mrs. Yeobright were to die, d\xe2\x80\x99ye think we should be took\r\nup and tried for the manslaughter of a woman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bring it in as that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam, \xe2\x80\x9cunless they could\r\nprove we had been poachers at some time of our lives. But she\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch\r\nround.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, if I had been stung by ten adders I should hardly have lost a\r\nday\xe2\x80\x99s work for\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Grandfer Cantle. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch is my spirit when I am\r\non my mettle. But perhaps \xe2\x80\x99tis natural in a man trained for war. Yes,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve gone through a good deal; but nothing ever came amiss to me after\r\nI joined the Locals in four.\xe2\x80\x9d He shook his head and smiled at a mental\r\npicture of himself in uniform. \xe2\x80\x9cI was always first in the most\r\ngalliantest scrapes in my younger days!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose that was because they always used to put the biggest fool\r\nafore,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway from the fire, beside which he knelt, blowing it\r\nwith his breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cD\xe2\x80\x99ye think so, Timothy?\xe2\x80\x9d said Grandfer Cantle, coming forward to\r\nFairway\xe2\x80\x99s side with sudden depression in his face. \xe2\x80\x9cThen a man may feel\r\nfor years that he is good solid company, and be wrong about himself\r\nafter all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind that question, Grandfer. Stir your stumps and get some more\r\nsticks. \xe2\x80\x99Tis very nonsense of an old man to prattle so when life and\r\ndeath\xe2\x80\x99s in mangling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Grandfer Cantle, with melancholy conviction. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\r\nthis is a bad night altogether for them that have done well in their\r\ntime; and if I were ever such a dab at the hautboy or tenor viol, I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have the heart to play tunes upon \xe2\x80\x99em now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSusan now arrived with the frying pan, when the live adder was killed\r\nand the heads of the three taken off. The remainders, being cut into\r\nlengths and split open, were tossed into the pan, which began hissing\r\nand crackling over the fire. Soon a rill of clear oil trickled from the\r\ncarcases, whereupon Clym dipped the corner of his handkerchief into the\r\nliquid and anointed the wound.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nVIII.\r\nEustacia Hears of Good Fortune, and Beholds Evil\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the meantime Eustacia, left alone in her cottage at Alderworth, had\r\nbecome considerably depressed by the posture of affairs. The\r\nconsequences which might result from Clym\xe2\x80\x99s discovery that his mother\r\nhad been turned from his door that day were likely to be disagreeable,\r\nand this was a quality in events which she hated as much as the\r\ndreadful.\r\n\r\nTo be left to pass the evening by herself was irksome to her at any\r\ntime, and this evening it was more irksome than usual by reason of the\r\nexcitements of the past hours. The two visits had stirred her into\r\nrestlessness. She was not wrought to any great pitch of uneasiness by\r\nthe probability of appearing in an ill light in the discussion between\r\nClym and his mother, but she was wrought to vexation, and her\r\nslumbering activities were quickened to the extent of wishing that she\r\nhad opened the door. She had certainly believed that Clym was awake,\r\nand the excuse would be an honest one as far as it went; but nothing\r\ncould save her from censure in refusing to answer at the first knock.\r\nYet, instead of blaming herself for the issue she laid the fault upon\r\nthe shoulders of some indistinct, colossal Prince of the World, who had\r\nframed her situation and ruled her lot.\r\n\r\nAt this time of the year it was pleasanter to walk by night than by\r\nday, and when Clym had been absent about an hour she suddenly resolved\r\nto go out in the direction of Blooms-End, on the chance of meeting him\r\non his return. When she reached the garden gate she heard wheels\r\napproaching, and looking round beheld her grandfather coming up in his\r\ncar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t stay a minute, thank ye,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered to her greeting. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\ndriving to East Egdon; but I came round here just to tell you the news.\r\nPerhaps you have heard\xe2\x80\x94about Mr. Wildeve\xe2\x80\x99s fortune?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustacia blankly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he has come into a fortune of eleven thousand pounds\xe2\x80\x94uncle died\r\nin Canada, just after hearing that all his family, whom he was sending\r\nhome, had gone to the bottom in the Cassiopeia; so Wildeve has come\r\ninto everything, without in the least expecting it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia stood motionless awhile. \xe2\x80\x9cHow long has he known of this?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it was known to him this morning early, for I knew it at ten\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, when Charley came back. Now, he is what I call a lucky man.\r\nWhat a fool you were, Eustacia!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn what way?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, lifting her eyes in apparent calmness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, in not sticking to him when you had him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad him, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not know there had ever been anything between you till lately;\r\nand, faith, I should have been hot and strong against it if I had\r\nknown; but since it seems that there was some sniffing between ye, why\r\nthe deuce didn\xe2\x80\x99t you stick to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia made no reply, but she looked as if she could say as much upon\r\nthat subject as he if she chose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how is your poor purblind husband?\xe2\x80\x9d continued the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cNot a\r\nbad fellow either, as far as he goes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is quite well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a good thing for his cousin what-d\xe2\x80\x99ye-call-her? By George, you\r\nought to have been in that galley, my girl! Now I must drive on. Do you\r\nwant any assistance? What\xe2\x80\x99s mine is yours, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Grandfather, we are not in want at present,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\ncoldly. \xe2\x80\x9cClym cuts furze, but he does it mostly as a useful pastime,\r\nbecause he can do nothing else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is paid for his pastime, isn\xe2\x80\x99t he? Three shillings a hundred, I\r\nheard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClym has money,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, colouring, \xe2\x80\x9cbut he likes to earn a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; good night.\xe2\x80\x9d And the captain drove on.\r\n\r\nWhen her grandfather was gone Eustacia went on her way mechanically;\r\nbut her thoughts were no longer concerning her mother-in-law and Clym.\r\nWildeve, notwithstanding his complaints against his fate, had been\r\nseized upon by destiny and placed in the sunshine once more. Eleven\r\nthousand pounds! From every Egdon point of view he was a rich man. In\r\nEustacia\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, too, it was an ample sum\xe2\x80\x94one sufficient to supply\r\nthose wants of hers which had been stigmatized by Clym in his more\r\naustere moods as vain and luxurious. Though she was no lover of money\r\nshe loved what money could bring; and the new accessories she imagined\r\naround him clothed Wildeve with a great deal of interest. She\r\nrecollected now how quietly well-dressed he had been that morning\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nhad probably put on his newest suit, regardless of damage by briars and\r\nthorns. And then she thought of his manner towards herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO I see it, I see it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cHow much he wishes he had me now,\r\nthat he might give me all I desire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn recalling the details of his glances and words\xe2\x80\x94at the time scarcely\r\nregarded\xe2\x80\x94it became plain to her how greatly they had been dictated by\r\nhis knowledge of this new event. \xe2\x80\x9cHad he been a man to bear a jilt\r\nill-will he would have told me of his good fortune in crowing tones;\r\ninstead of doing that he mentioned not a word, in deference to my\r\nmisfortunes, and merely implied that he loved me still, as one superior\r\nto him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWildeve\xe2\x80\x99s silence that day on what had happened to him was just the\r\nkind of behaviour calculated to make an impression on such a woman.\r\nThose delicate touches of good taste were, in fact, one of the strong\r\npoints in his demeanour towards the other sex. The peculiarity of\r\nWildeve was that, while at one time passionate, upbraiding, and\r\nresentful towards a woman, at another he would treat her with such\r\nunparalleled grace as to make previous neglect appear as no\r\ndiscourtesy, injury as no insult, interference as a delicate attention,\r\nand the ruin of her honour as excess of chivalry. This man, whose\r\nadmiration today Eustacia had disregarded, whose good wishes she had\r\nscarcely taken the trouble to accept, whom she had shown out of the\r\nhouse by the back door, was the possessor of eleven thousand pounds\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nman of fair professional education, and one who had served his articles\r\nwith a civil engineer.\r\n\r\nSo intent was Eustacia upon Wildeve\xe2\x80\x99s fortunes that she forgot how much\r\ncloser to her own course were those of Clym; and instead of walking on\r\nto meet him at once she sat down upon a stone. She was disturbed in her\r\nreverie by a voice behind, and turning her head beheld the old lover\r\nand fortunate inheritor of wealth immediately beside her.\r\n\r\nShe remained sitting, though the fluctuation in her look might have\r\ntold any man who knew her so well as Wildeve that she was thinking of\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you come here?\xe2\x80\x9d she said in her clear low tone. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought you\r\nwere at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went on to the village after leaving your garden; and now I have\r\ncome back again\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all. Which way are you walking, may I ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe waved her hand in the direction of Blooms-End. \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to meet\r\nmy husband. I think I may possibly have got into trouble whilst you\r\nwere with me today.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could that be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy not letting in Mrs. Yeobright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope that visit of mine did you no harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone. It was not your fault,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quietly.\r\n\r\nBy this time she had risen; and they involuntarily sauntered on\r\ntogether, without speaking, for two or three minutes; when Eustacia\r\nbroke silence by saying, \xe2\x80\x9cI assume I must congratulate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn what? O yes; on my eleven thousand pounds, you mean. Well, since I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t get something else, I must be content with getting that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem very indifferent about it. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me today when\r\nyou came?\xe2\x80\x9d she said in the tone of a neglected person. \xe2\x80\x9cI heard of it\r\nquite by accident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did mean to tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x94well, I will speak\r\nfrankly\xe2\x80\x94I did not like to mention it when I saw, Eustacia, that your\r\nstar was not high. The sight of a man lying wearied out with hard work,\r\nas your husband lay, made me feel that to brag of my own fortune to you\r\nwould be greatly out of place. Yet, as you stood there beside him, I\r\ncould not help feeling too that in many respects he was a richer man\r\nthan I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this Eustacia said, with slumbering mischievousness, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat, would\r\nyou exchange with him\xe2\x80\x94your fortune for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly would,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs we are imagining what is impossible and absurd, suppose we change\r\nthe subject?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; and I will tell you of my plans for the future, if you care\r\nto hear them. I shall permanently invest nine thousand pounds, keep one\r\nthousand as ready money, and with the remaining thousand travel for a\r\nyear or so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTravel? What a bright idea! Where will you go to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom here to Paris, where I shall pass the winter and spring. Then I\r\nshall go to Italy, Greece, Egypt, and Palestine, before the hot weather\r\ncomes on. In the summer I shall go to America; and then, by a plan not\r\nyet settled, I shall go to Australia and round to India. By that time I\r\nshall have begun to have had enough of it. Then I shall probably come\r\nback to Paris again, and there I shall stay as long as I can afford\r\nto.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBack to Paris again,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured in a voice that was nearly a sigh.\r\nShe had never once told Wildeve of the Parisian desires which Clym\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndescription had sown in her; yet here was he involuntarily in a\r\nposition to gratify them. \xe2\x80\x9cYou think a good deal of Paris?\xe2\x80\x9d she added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. In my opinion it is the central beauty-spot of the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd in mine! And Thomasin will go with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if she cares to. She may prefer to stay at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you will be going about, and I shall be staying here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you will. But we know whose fault that is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not blaming you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I thought you were. If ever you _should_ be inclined to blame me,\r\nthink of a certain evening by Rainbarrow, when you promised to meet me\r\nand did not. You sent me a letter; and my heart ached to read that as I\r\nhope yours never will. That was one point of divergence. I then did\r\nsomething in haste.... But she is a good woman, and I will say no\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that the blame was on my side that time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustacia. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nit had not always been so. However, it is my misfortune to be too\r\nsudden in feeling. O, Damon, don\xe2\x80\x99t reproach me any more\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t bear\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went on silently for a distance of two or three miles, when\r\nEustacia said suddenly, \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you come out of your way, Mr.\r\nWildeve?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy way is anywhere tonight. I will go with you as far as the hill on\r\nwhich we can see Blooms-End, as it is getting late for you to be\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t trouble. I am not obliged to be out at all. I think I would\r\nrather you did not accompany me further. This sort of thing would have\r\nan odd look if known.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, I will leave you.\xe2\x80\x9d He took her hand unexpectedly, and\r\nkissed it\xe2\x80\x94for the first time since her marriage. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat light is that on\r\nthe hill?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as it were to hide the caress.\r\n\r\nShe looked, and saw a flickering firelight proceeding from the open\r\nside of a hovel a little way before them. The hovel, which she had\r\nhitherto always found empty, seemed to be inhabited now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince you have come so far,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustacia, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you see me safely\r\npast that hut? I thought I should have met Clym somewhere about here,\r\nbut as he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t appear I will hasten on and get to Blooms-End before\r\nhe leaves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey advanced to the turf-shed, and when they got near it the firelight\r\nand the lantern inside showed distinctly enough the form of a woman\r\nreclining on a bed of fern, a group of heath men and women standing\r\naround her. Eustacia did not recognize Mrs. Yeobright in the reclining\r\nfigure, nor Clym as one of the standers-by till she came close. Then\r\nshe quickly pressed her hand up on Wildeve\xe2\x80\x99s arm and signified to him\r\nto come back from the open side of the shed into the shadow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my husband and his mother,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered in an agitated voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can it mean? Will you step forward and tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWildeve left her side and went to the back wall of the hut. Presently\r\nEustacia perceived that he was beckoning to her, and she advanced and\r\njoined him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a serious case,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve.\r\n\r\nFrom their position they could hear what was proceeding inside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot think where she could have been going,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym to someone.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe had evidently walked a long way, but even when she was able to\r\nspeak just now she would not tell me where. What do you really think of\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a great deal to fear,\xe2\x80\x9d was gravely answered, in a voice which\r\nEustacia recognized as that of the only surgeon in the district. \xe2\x80\x9cShe\r\nhas suffered somewhat from the bite of the adder; but it is exhaustion\r\nwhich has overpowered her. My impression is that her walk must have\r\nbeen exceptionally long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI used to tell her not to overwalk herself this weather,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym,\r\nwith distress. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think we did well in using the adder\xe2\x80\x99s fat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is a very ancient remedy\xe2\x80\x94the old remedy of the\r\nviper-catchers, I believe,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the doctor. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is mentioned as an\r\ninfallible ointment by Hoffman, Mead, and I think the Abb\xc3\xa9 Fontana.\r\nUndoubtedly it was as good a thing as you could do; though I question\r\nif some other oils would not have been equally efficacious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here, come here!\xe2\x80\x9d was then rapidly said in anxious female tones,\r\nand Clym and the doctor could be heard rushing forward from the back\r\npart of the shed to where Mrs. Yeobright lay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Eustacia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Twas Thomasin who spoke,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve. \xe2\x80\x9cThen they have fetched her.\r\nI wonder if I had better go in\xe2\x80\x94yet it might do harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a long time there was utter silence among the group within; and it\r\nwas broken at last by Clym saying, in an agonized voice, \xe2\x80\x9cO Doctor,\r\nwhat does it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe doctor did not reply at once; ultimately he said, \xe2\x80\x9cShe is sinking\r\nfast. Her heart was previously affected, and physical exhaustion has\r\ndealt the finishing blow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen there was a weeping of women, then waiting, then hushed\r\nexclamations, then a strange gasping sound, then a painful stillness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is all over,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor.\r\n\r\nFurther back in the hut the cotters whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Yeobright is\r\ndead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlmost at the same moment the two watchers observed the form of a small\r\nold-fashioned child entering at the open side of the shed. Susan\r\nNunsuch, whose boy it was, went forward to the opening and silently\r\nbeckoned to him to go back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got something to tell \xe2\x80\x99ee, Mother,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried in a shrill tone.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat woman asleep there walked along with me today; and she said I was\r\nto say that I had seed her, and she was a broken-hearted woman and cast\r\noff by her son, and then I came on home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA confused sob as from a man was heard within, upon which Eustacia\r\ngasped faintly, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Clym\xe2\x80\x94I must go to him\xe2\x80\x94yet dare I do it? No\xe2\x80\x94come\r\naway!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they had withdrawn from the neighbourhood of the shed she said\r\nhuskily, \xe2\x80\x9cI am to blame for this. There is evil in store for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas she not admitted to your house after all?\xe2\x80\x9d Wildeve inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, and that\xe2\x80\x99s where it all lies! Oh, what shall I do! I shall not\r\nintrude upon them\xe2\x80\x94I shall go straight home. Damon, good-bye! I cannot\r\nspeak to you any more now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey parted company; and when Eustacia had reached the next hill she\r\nlooked back. A melancholy procession was wending its way by the light\r\nof the lantern from the hut towards Blooms-End. Wildeve was nowhere to\r\nbe seen.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nBOOK FIFTH\xe2\x80\x94THE DISCOVERY\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nI.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWherefore Is Light Given to Him That Is in Misery\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nOne evening, about three weeks after the funeral of Mrs. Yeobright,\r\nwhen the silver face of the moon sent a bundle of beams directly upon\r\nthe floor of Clym\xe2\x80\x99s house at Alderworth, a woman came forth from\r\nwithin. She reclined over the garden gate as if to refresh herself\r\nawhile. The pale lunar touches which make beauties of hags lent\r\ndivinity to this face, already beautiful.\r\n\r\nShe had not long been there when a man came up the road and with some\r\nhesitation said to her, \xe2\x80\x9cHow is he tonight, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, if you please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is better, though still very unwell, Humphrey,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Eustacia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he light-headed, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He is quite sensible now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo he rave about his mother just the same, poor fellow?\xe2\x80\x9d continued\r\nHumphrey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as much, though not quite so wildly,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a low voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was very unfortunate, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, that the boy Johnny should ever ha\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntold him his mother\xe2\x80\x99s dying words, about her being broken-hearted and\r\ncast off by her son. \xe2\x80\x99Twas enough to upset any man alive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia made no reply beyond that of a slight catch in her breath, as\r\nof one who fain would speak but could not; and Humphrey, declining her\r\ninvitation to come in, went away.\r\n\r\nEustacia turned, entered the house, and ascended to the front bedroom,\r\nwhere a shaded light was burning. In the bed lay Clym, pale, haggard,\r\nwide awake, tossing to one side and to the other, his eyes lit by a hot\r\nlight, as if the fire in their pupils were burning up their substance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it you, Eustacia?\xe2\x80\x9d he said as she sat down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Clym. I have been down to the gate. The moon is shining\r\nbeautifully, and there is not a leaf stirring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShining, is it? What\xe2\x80\x99s the moon to a man like me? Let it shine\xe2\x80\x94let\r\nanything be, so that I never see another day!... Eustacia, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhere to look\xe2\x80\x94my thoughts go through me like swords. O, if any man\r\nwants to make himself immortal by painting a picture of wretchedness,\r\nlet him come here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you say so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot help feeling that I did my best to kill her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Clym.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it was so; it is useless to excuse me! My conduct to her was too\r\nhideous\xe2\x80\x94I made no advances; and she could not bring herself to forgive\r\nme. Now she is dead! If I had only shown myself willing to make it up\r\nwith her sooner, and we had been friends, and then she had died, it\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so hard to bear. But I never went near her house, so she\r\nnever came near mine, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how welcome she would have\r\nbeen\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what troubles me. She did not know I was going to her house\r\nthat very night, for she was too insensible to understand me. If she\r\nhad only come to see me! I longed that she would. But it was not to\r\nbe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere escaped from Eustacia one of those shivering sighs which used to\r\nshake her like a pestilent blast. She had not yet told.\r\n\r\nBut Yeobright was too deeply absorbed in the ramblings incidental to\r\nhis remorseful state to notice her. During his illness he had been\r\ncontinually talking thus. Despair had been added to his original grief\r\nby the unfortunate disclosure of the boy who had received the last\r\nwords of Mrs. Yeobright\xe2\x80\x94words too bitterly uttered in an hour of\r\nmisapprehension. Then his distress had overwhelmed him, and he longed\r\nfor death as a field labourer longs for the shade. It was the pitiful\r\nsight of a man standing in the very focus of sorrow. He continually\r\nbewailed his tardy journey to his mother\xe2\x80\x99s house, because it was an\r\nerror which could never be rectified, and insisted that he must have\r\nbeen horribly perverted by some fiend not to have thought before that\r\nit was his duty to go to her, since she did not come to him. He would\r\nask Eustacia to agree with him in his self-condemnation; and when she,\r\nseared inwardly by a secret she dared not tell, declared that she could\r\nnot give an opinion, he would say, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s because you didn\xe2\x80\x99t know my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s nature. She was always ready to forgive if asked to do so; but\r\nI seemed to her to be as an obstinate child, and that made her\r\nunyielding. Yet not unyielding\xe2\x80\x94she was proud and reserved, no more....\r\nYes, I can understand why she held out against me so long. She was\r\nwaiting for me. I dare say she said a hundred times in her sorrow,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What a return he makes for all the sacrifices I have made for him!\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nnever went to her! When I set out to visit her it was too late. To\r\nthink of that is nearly intolerable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSometimes his condition had been one of utter remorse, unsoftened by a\r\nsingle tear of pure sorrow: and then he writhed as he lay, fevered far\r\nmore by thought than by physical ills. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I could only get one\r\nassurance that she did not die in a belief that I was resentful,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid one day when in this mood, \xe2\x80\x9cit would be better to think of than a\r\nhope of heaven. But that I cannot do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou give yourself up too much to this wearying despair,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nEustacia. \xe2\x80\x9cOther men\xe2\x80\x99s mothers have died.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make the loss of mine less. Yet it is less the loss than\r\nthe circumstances of the loss. I sinned against her, and on that\r\naccount there is no light for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe sinned against you, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, she did not. I committed the guilt; and may the whole burden be\r\nupon my head!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you might consider twice before you say that,\xe2\x80\x9d Eustacia\r\nreplied. \xe2\x80\x9cSingle men have, no doubt, a right to curse themselves as\r\nmuch as they please; but men with wives involve two in the doom they\r\npray down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am in too sorry a state to understand what you are refining on,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the wretched man. \xe2\x80\x9cDay and night shout at me, \xe2\x80\x98You have helped to\r\nkill her.\xe2\x80\x99 But in loathing myself I may, I own, be unjust to you, my\r\npoor wife. Forgive me for it, Eustacia, for I scarcely know what I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEustacia was always anxious to avoid the sight of her husband in such a\r\nstate as this, which had become as dreadful to her as the trial scene\r\nwas to Judas Iscariot. It brought before her eyes the spectre of a\r\nworn-out woman knocking at a door which she would not open; and she\r\nshrank from contemplating it. Yet it was better for Yeobright himself\r\nwhen he spoke openly of his sharp regret, for in silence he endured\r\ninfinitely more, and would sometimes remain so long in a tense,\r\nbrooding mood, consuming himself by the gnawing of his thought, that it\r\nwas imperatively necessary to make him talk aloud, that his grief might\r\nin some degree expend itself in the effort.\r\n\r\nEustacia had not been long indoors after her look at the moonlight when\r\na soft footstep came up to the house, and Thomasin was announced by the\r\nwoman downstairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Thomasin! Thank you for coming tonight,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym when she\r\nentered the room. \xe2\x80\x9cHere am I, you see. Such a wretched spectacle am I,\r\nthat I shrink from being seen by a single friend, and almost from you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not shrink from me, dear Clym,\xe2\x80\x9d said Thomasin earnestly, in\r\nthat sweet voice of hers which came to a sufferer like fresh air into a\r\nBlack Hole. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing in you can ever shock me or drive me away. I have\r\nbeen here before, but you don\xe2\x80\x99t remember it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do; I am not delirious, Thomasin, nor have I been so at all.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you believe that if they say so. I am only in great misery at\r\nwhat I have done, and that, with the weakness, makes me seem mad. But\r\nit has not upset my reason. Do you think I should remember all about my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s death if I were out of my mind? No such good luck. Two months\r\nand a half, Thomasin, the last of her life, did my poor mother live\r\nalone, distracted and mourning because of me; yet she was unvisited by\r\nme, though I was living only six miles off. Two months and a\r\nhalf\xe2\x80\x94seventy-five days did the sun rise and set upon her in that\r\ndeserted state which a dog didn\xe2\x80\x99t deserve! Poor people who had nothing\r\nin common with her would have cared for her, and visited her had they\r\nknown her sickness and loneliness; but I, who should have been all to\r\nher, stayed away like a cur. If there is any justice in God let Him\r\nkill me now. He has nearly blinded me, but that is not enough. If He\r\nwould only strike me with more pain I would believe in Him forever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush, hush! O, pray, Clym, don\xe2\x80\x99t, don\xe2\x80\x99t say it!\xe2\x80\x9d implored Thomasin,\r\naffrighted into sobs and tears; while Eustacia, at the other side of\r\nthe room, though her pale face remained calm, writhed in her chair.\r\nClym went on without heeding his cousin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I am not worth receiving further proof even of Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreprobation. Do you think, Thomasin, that she knew me\xe2\x80\x94that she did not\r\ndie in that horrid mistaken notion about my not forgiving her, which I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t tell you how she acquired? If you could only assure me of that!\r\nDo you think so, Eustacia? Do speak to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I can assure you that she knew better at last,\xe2\x80\x9d said Thomasin.\r\nThe pallid Eustacia said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t she come to my house? I would have taken her in and showed\r\nher how I loved her in spite of all. But she never came; and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngo to her, and she died on the heath like an animal kicked out, nobody\r\nto help her till it was too late. If you could have seen her, Thomasin,\r\nas I saw her\xe2\x80\x94a poor dying woman, lying in the dark upon the bare\r\nground, moaning, nobody near, believing she was utterly deserted by all\r\nthe world, it would have moved you to anguish, it would have moved a\r\nbrute. And this poor woman my mother! No wonder she said to the child,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You have seen a broken-hearted woman.\xe2\x80\x99 What a state she must have been\r\nbrought to, to say that! and who can have done it but I? It is too\r\ndreadful to think of, and I wish I could be punished more heavily than\r\nI am. How long was I what they called out of my senses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA week, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then I became calm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, for four days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now I have left off being calm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut try to be quiet\xe2\x80\x94please do, and you will soon be strong. If you\r\ncould remove that impression from your mind\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to get strong.\r\nWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the use of my getting well? It would be better for me if I die,\r\nand it would certainly be better for Eustacia. Is Eustacia there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be better for you, Eustacia, if I were to die?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t press such a question, dear Clym.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it really is but a shadowy supposition; for unfortunately I am\r\ngoing to live. I feel myself getting better. Thomasin, how long are you\r\ngoing to stay at the inn, now that all this money has come to your\r\nhusband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother month or two, probably; until my illness is over. We cannot\r\nget off till then. I think it will be a month or more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes. Of course. Ah, Cousin Tamsie, you will get over your\r\ntrouble\xe2\x80\x94one little month will take you through it, and bring something\r\nto console you; but I shall never get over mine, and no consolation\r\nwill come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClym, you are unjust to yourself. Depend upon it, Aunt thought kindly\r\nof you. I know that, if she had lived, you would have been reconciled\r\nwith her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she didn\xe2\x80\x99t come to see me, though I asked her, before I married,\r\nif she would come. Had she come, or had I gone there, she would never\r\nhave died saying, \xe2\x80\x98I am a broken-hearted woman, cast off by my son.\xe2\x80\x99 My\r\ndoor has always been open to her\xe2\x80\x94a welcome here has always awaited her.\r\nBut that she never came to see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better not talk any more now, Clym,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustacia faintly\r\nfrom the other part of the room, for the scene was growing intolerable\r\nto her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me talk to you instead for the little time I shall be here,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThomasin said soothingly. \xe2\x80\x9cConsider what a one-sided way you have of\r\nlooking at the matter, Clym. When she said that to the little boy you\r\nhad not found her and taken her into your arms; and it might have been\r\nuttered in a moment of bitterness. It was rather like Aunt to say\r\nthings in haste. She sometimes used to speak so to me. Though she did\r\nnot come I am convinced that she thought of coming to see you. Do you\r\nsuppose a man\xe2\x80\x99s mother could live two or three months without one\r\nforgiving thought? She forgave me; and why should she not have forgiven\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou laboured to win her round; I did nothing. I, who was going to\r\nteach people the higher secrets of happiness, did not know how to keep\r\nout of that gross misery which the most untaught are wise enough to\r\navoid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you get here tonight, Thomasin?\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustacia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamon set me down at the end of the lane. He has driven into East\r\nEgdon on business, and he will come and pick me up by-and-by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAccordingly they soon after heard the noise of wheels. Wildeve had\r\ncome, and was waiting outside with his horse and gig.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSend out and tell him I will be down in two minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Thomasin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will run down myself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eustacia.\r\n\r\nShe went down. Wildeve had alighted, and was standing before the\r\nhorse\xe2\x80\x99s head when Eustacia opened the door. He did not turn for a\r\nmoment, thinking the comer Thomasin. Then he looked, startled ever so\r\nlittle, and said one word: \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not yet told him,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen don\xe2\x80\x99t do so till he is well\xe2\x80\x94it will be fatal. You are ill\r\nyourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am wretched.... O Damon,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, bursting into tears, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntell you how unhappy I am! I can hardly bear this. I can tell nobody of\r\nmy trouble\xe2\x80\x94nobody knows of it but you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor girl!\xe2\x80\x9d said Wildeve, visibly affected at her distress, and at\r\nlast led on so far as to take her hand. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is hard, when you have done\r\nnothing to deserve it, that you should have got involved in such a web\r\nas this. You were not made for these sad scenes. I am to blame most. If\r\nI could only have saved you from it all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Damon, please pray tell me what I must do? To sit by him hour\r\nafter hour, and hear him reproach himself as being the cause of her\r\ndeath, and to know that I am the sinner, if any human being is at all,\r\ndrives me into cold despair. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do. Should I tell him\r\nor should I not tell him? I always am asking myself that. O, I want to\r\ntell him; and yet I am afraid. If he finds it out he must surely kill\r\nme, for nothing else will be in proportion to his feelings now. \xe2\x80\x98Beware\r\nthe fury of a patient man\xe2\x80\x99 sounds day by day in my ears as I watch\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, wait till he is better, and trust to chance. And when you tell,\r\nyou must only tell part\xe2\x80\x94for his own sake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich part should I keep back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWildeve paused. \xe2\x80\x9cThat I was in the house at the time,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a low\r\ntone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; it must be concealed, seeing what has been whispered. How much\r\neasier are hasty actions than speeches that will excuse them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he were only to die\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Wildeve murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not think of it! I would not buy hope of immunity by so cowardly a\r\ndesire even if I hated him. Now I am going up to him again. Thomasin\r\nbade me tell you she would be down in a few minutes. Good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe returned, and Thomasin soon appeared. When she was seated in the\r\ngig with her husband, and the horse was turning to go off, Wildeve\r\nlifted his eyes to the bedroom windows. Looking from one of them he\r\ncould discern a pale, tragic face watching him drive away. It was\r\nEustacia\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nII.\r\nA Lurid Light Breaks in upon a Darkened Understanding\r\n\r\n\r\nClym\xe2\x80\x99s grief became mitigated by wearing itself out. His strength\r\nreturned, and a month after the visit of Thomasin he might have been\r\nseen walking about the garden. Endurance and despair, equanimity and\r\ngloom, the tints of health and the pallor of death, mingled weirdly in\r\nhis face. He was now unnaturally silent upon all of the past that\r\nrelated to his mother; and though Eustacia knew that he was thinking of\r\nit none the less, she was only too glad to escape the topic ever to\r\nbring it up anew. When his mind had been weaker his heart had led him\r\nto speak out; but reason having now somewhat recovered itself he sank\r\ninto taciturnity.\r\n\r\nOne evening when he was thus standing in the garden, abstractedly\r\nspudding up a weed with his stick, a bony figure turned the corner of\r\nthe house and came up to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristian, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym. \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad you have found me out. I\r\nshall soon want you to go to Blooms-End and assist me in putting the\r\nhouse in order. I suppose it is all locked up as I left it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mister Clym.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you dug up the potatoes and other roots?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, without a drop o\xe2\x80\x99 rain, thank God. But I was coming to tell \xe2\x80\x99ee\r\nof something else which is quite different from what we have lately had\r\nin the family. I am sent by the rich gentleman at the Woman, that we\r\nused to call the landlord, to tell \xe2\x80\x99ee that Mrs. Wildeve is doing well\r\nof a girl, which was born punctually at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock at noon, or a few\r\nminutes more or less; and \xe2\x80\x99tis said that expecting of this increase is\r\nwhat have kept \xe2\x80\x99em there since they came into their money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she is getting on well, you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. Only Mr. Wildeve is twanky because \xe2\x80\x99tisn\xe2\x80\x99t a boy\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\nthey say in the kitchen, but I was not supposed to notice that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristian, now listen to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sure, Mr. Yeobright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see my mother the day before she died?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I did not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYeobright\xe2\x80\x99s face expressed disappointment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I zeed her the morning of the same day she died.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym\xe2\x80\x99s look lighted up. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s nearer still to my meaning,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know \xe2\x80\x99twas the same day; for she said, \xe2\x80\x98I be going to see him,\r\nChristian; so I shall not want any vegetables brought in for dinner.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee whom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee you. She was going to your house, you understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYeobright regarded Christian with intense surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you never\r\nmention this?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure it was my house she was coming\r\nto?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO yes. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mention it because I\xe2\x80\x99ve never zeed you lately. And as\r\nshe didn\xe2\x80\x99t get there it was all nought, and nothing to tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I have been wondering why she should have walked in the heath on\r\nthat hot day! Well, did she say what she was coming for? It is a thing,\r\nChristian, I am very anxious to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mister Clym. She didn\xe2\x80\x99t say it to me, though I think she did to\r\none here and there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know one person to whom she spoke of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is one man, please, sir, but I hope you won\xe2\x80\x99t mention my name to\r\nhim, as I have seen him in strange places, particular in dreams. One\r\nnight last summer he glared at me like Famine and Sword, and it made me\r\nfeel so low that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t comb out my few hairs for two days. He was\r\nstanding, as it might be, Mister Yeobright, in the middle of the path\r\nto Mistover, and your mother came up, looking as pale\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, when was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast summer, in my dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooh! Who\xe2\x80\x99s the man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiggory, the reddleman. He called upon her and sat with her the\r\nevening before she set out to see you. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t gone home from work\r\nwhen he came up to the gate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must see Venn\xe2\x80\x94I wish I had known it before,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym anxiously. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwonder why he has not come to tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe went out of Egdon Heath the next day, so would not be likely to\r\nknow you wanted him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChristian,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must go and find Venn. I am otherwise\r\nengaged, or I would go myself. Find him at once, and tell him I want to\r\nspeak to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a good hand at hunting up folk by day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Christian, looking\r\ndubiously round at the declining light; \xe2\x80\x9cbut as to night-time, never is\r\nsuch a bad hand as I, Mister Yeobright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSearch the heath when you will, so that you bring him soon. Bring him\r\ntomorrow, if you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChristian then departed. The morrow came, but no Venn. In the evening\r\nChristian arrived, looking very weary. He had been searching all day,\r\nand had heard nothing of the reddleman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInquire as much as you can tomorrow without neglecting your work,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Yeobright. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t come again till you have found him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next day Yeobright set out for the old house at Blooms-End, which,\r\nwith the garden, was now his own. His severe illness had hindered all\r\npreparations for his removal thither; but it had become necessary that\r\nhe should go and overlook its contents, as administrator to his\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s little property; for which purpose he decided to pass the next\r\nnight on the premises.\r\n\r\nHe journeyed onward, not quickly or decisively, but in the slow walk of\r\none who has been awakened from a stupefying sleep. It was early\r\nafternoon when he reached the valley. The expression of the place, the\r\ntone of the hour, were precisely those of many such occasions in days\r\ngone by; and these antecedent similarities fostered the illusion that\r\nshe, who was there no longer, would come out to welcome him. The garden\r\ngate was locked and the shutters were closed, just as he himself had\r\nleft them on the evening after the funeral. He unlocked the gate, and\r\nfound that a spider had already constructed a large web, tying the door\r\nto the lintel, on the supposition that it was never to be opened again.\r\nWhen he had entered the house and flung back the shutters he set about\r\nhis task of overhauling the cupboards and closets, burning papers, and\r\nconsidering how best to arrange the place for Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s reception,\r\nuntil such time as he might be in a position to carry out his\r\nlong-delayed scheme, should that time ever arrive.\r\n\r\nAs he surveyed the rooms he felt strongly disinclined for the\r\nalterations which would have to be made in the time-honoured furnishing\r\nof his parents and grandparents, to suit Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s modern ideas. The\r\ngaunt oak-cased clock, with the picture of the Ascension on the door\r\npanel and the Miraculous Draught of Fishes on the base; his\r\ngrandmother\xe2\x80\x99s corner cupboard with the glass door, through which the\r\nspotted china was visible; the dumb-waiter; the wooden tea trays; the\r\nhanging fountain with the brass tap\xe2\x80\x94whither would these venerable\r\narticles have to be banished?\r\n\r\nHe noticed that the flowers in the window had died for want of water,\r\nand he placed them out upon the ledge, that they might be taken away.\r\nWhile thus engaged he heard footsteps on the gravel without, and\r\nsomebody knocked at the door.\r\n\r\nYeobright opened it, and Venn was standing before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning,\xe2\x80\x9d said the reddleman. \xe2\x80\x9cIs Mrs. Yeobright at home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYeobright looked upon the ground. \xe2\x80\x9cThen you have not seen Christian or\r\nany of the Egdon folks?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I have only just returned after a long stay away. I called here\r\nthe day before I left.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have heard nothing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother is\xe2\x80\x94dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDead!\xe2\x80\x9d said Venn mechanically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer home now is where I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind having mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVenn regarded him, and then said, \xe2\x80\x9cIf I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see your face I could\r\nnever believe your words. Have you been ill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had an illness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the change! When I parted from her a month ago everything seemed\r\nto say that she was going to begin a new life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what seemed came true.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say right, no doubt. Trouble has taught you a deeper vein of talk\r\nthan mine. All I meant was regarding her life here. She has died too\r\nsoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps through my living too long. I have had a bitter experience on\r\nthat score this last month, Diggory. But come in; I have been wanting\r\nto see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe conducted the reddleman into the large room where the dancing had\r\ntaken place the previous Christmas, and they sat down in the settle\r\ntogether. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s the cold fireplace, you see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen that\r\nhalf-burnt log and those cinders were alight she was alive! Little has\r\nbeen changed here yet. I can do nothing. My life creeps like a snail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow came she to die?\xe2\x80\x9d said Venn.\r\n\r\nYeobright gave him some particulars of her illness and death, and\r\ncontinued: \xe2\x80\x9cAfter this no kind of pain will ever seem more than an\r\nindisposition to me. I began saying that I wanted to ask you something,\r\nbut I stray from subjects like a drunken man. I am anxious to know what\r\nmy mother said to you when she last saw you. You talked with her a long\r\ntime, I think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI talked with her more than half an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. And it must have been on account of what we said that she was on\r\nthe heath. Without question she was coming to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why should she come to see me if she felt so bitterly against me?\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s the mystery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet I know she quite forgave \xe2\x80\x99ee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Diggory\xe2\x80\x94would a woman, who had quite forgiven her son, say, when\r\nshe felt herself ill on the way to his house, that she was\r\nbroken-hearted because of his ill-usage? Never!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I know is that she didn\xe2\x80\x99t blame you at all. She blamed herself\r\nfor what had happened, and only herself. I had it from her own lips.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had it from her lips that I had _not_ ill-treated her; and at the\r\nsame time another had it from her lips that I _had_ ill-treated her? My\r\nmother was no impulsive woman who changed her opinion every hour\r\nwithout reason. How can it be, Venn, that she should have told such\r\ndifferent stories in close succession?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot say. It is certainly odd, when she had forgiven you, and had\r\nforgiven your wife, and was going to see ye on purpose to make\r\nfriends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there was one thing wanting to bewilder me it was this\r\nincomprehensible thing!... Diggory, if we, who remain alive, were only\r\nallowed to hold conversation with the dead\xe2\x80\x94just once, a bare minute,\r\neven through a screen of iron bars, as with persons in prison\xe2\x80\x94what we\r\nmight learn! How many who now ride smiling would hide their heads! And\r\nthis mystery\xe2\x80\x94I should then be at the bottom of it at once. But the\r\ngrave has forever shut her in; and how shall it be found out now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo reply was returned by his companion, since none could be given; and\r\nwhen Venn left, a few minutes later, Clym had passed from the dullness\r\nof sorrow to the fluctuation of carking incertitude.\r\n\r\nHe continued in the same state all the afternoon. A bed was made up for\r\nhim in the same house by a neighbour, that he might not have to return\r\nagain the next day; and when he retired to rest in the deserted place\r\nit was only to remain awake hour after hour thinking the same thoughts.\r\nHow to discover a solution to this riddle of death seemed a query of\r\nmore importance than highest problems of the living. There was housed\r\nin his memory a vivid picture of the face of a little boy as he entered\r\nthe hovel where Clym\xe2\x80\x99s mother lay. The round eyes, eager gaze, the\r\npiping voice which enunciated the words, had operated like stilettos on\r\nhis brain.\r\n\r\nA visit to the boy suggested itself as a means of gleaning new\r\nparticulars; though it might be quite unproductive. To probe a child\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmind after the lapse of six weeks, not for facts which the child had\r\nseen and understood, but to get at those which were in their nature\r\nbeyond him, did not promise much; yet when every obvious channel is\r\nblocked we grope towards the small and obscure. There was nothing else\r\nleft to do; after that he would allow the enigma to drop into the abyss\r\nof undiscoverable things.\r\n\r\nIt was about daybreak when he had reached this decision, and he at once\r\narose. He locked up the house and went out into the green patch which\r\nmerged in heather further on. In front of the white garden-palings the\r\npath branched into three like a broad arrow. The road to the right led\r\nto the Quiet Woman and its neighbourhood; the middle track led to\r\nMistover Knap; the left-hand track led over the hill to another part of\r\nMistover, where the child lived. On inclining into the latter path\r\nYeobright felt a creeping chilliness, familiar enough to most people,\r\nand probably caused by the unsunned morning air. In after days he\r\nthought of it as a thing of singular significance.\r\n\r\nWhen Yeobright reached the cottage of Susan Nunsuch, the mother of the\r\nboy he sought, he found that the inmates were not yet astir. But in\r\nupland hamlets the transition from a-bed to abroad is surprisingly\r\nswift and easy. There no dense partition of yawns and toilets divides\r\nhumanity by night from humanity by day. Yeobright tapped at the upper\r\nwindowsill, which he could reach with his walking stick; and in three\r\nor four minutes the woman came down.\r\n\r\nIt was not till this moment that Clym recollected her to be the person\r\nwho had behaved so barbarously to Eustacia. It partly explained the\r\ninsuavity with which the woman greeted him. Moreover, the boy had been\r\nailing again; and Susan now, as ever since the night when he had been\r\npressed into Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s service at the bonfire, attributed his\r\nindispositions to Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s influence as a witch.
