I have got so mixed up with business\r\nof one sort and t\xe2\x80\x99other that my soft sentiments are gone off in vapour\r\nlike. Yes, I am given up body and soul to the making of money. Money is\r\nall my dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Diggory, how wicked!\xe2\x80\x9d said Thomasin reproachfully, and looking at\r\nhim in exact balance between taking his words seriously and judging\r\nthem as said to tease her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, \xe2\x80\x99tis rather a rum course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Venn, in the bland tone of one\r\ncomfortably resigned to sins he could no longer overcome.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, who used to be so nice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s an argument I rather like, because what a man has once\r\nbeen he may be again.\xe2\x80\x9d Thomasin blushed. \xe2\x80\x9cExcept that it is rather\r\nharder now,\xe2\x80\x9d Venn continued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you be richer than you were at that time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no\xe2\x80\x94not much. I have made it nearly all over to the baby, as it was\r\nmy duty to do, except just enough to live on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am rather glad of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Venn softly, and regarding her from\r\nthe corner of his eye, \xe2\x80\x9cfor it makes it easier for us to be friendly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThomasin blushed again, and, when a few more words had been said of a\r\nnot unpleasing kind, Venn mounted his horse and rode on.\r\n\r\nThis conversation had passed in a hollow of the heath near the old\r\nRoman road, a place much frequented by Thomasin. And it might have been\r\nobserved that she did not in future walk that way less often from\r\nhaving met Venn there now. Whether or not Venn abstained from riding\r\nthither because he had met Thomasin in the same place might easily have\r\nbeen guessed from her proceedings about two months later in the same\r\nyear.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIII.\r\nThe Serious Discourse of Clym with His Cousin\r\n\r\n\r\nThroughout this period Yeobright had more or less pondered on his duty\r\nto his cousin Thomasin. He could not help feeling that it would be a\r\npitiful waste of sweet material if the tender-natured thing should be\r\ndoomed from this early stage of her life onwards to dribble away her\r\nwinsome qualities on lonely gorse and fern. But he felt this as an\r\neconomist merely, and not as a lover. His passion for Eustacia had been\r\na sort of conserve of his whole life, and he had nothing more of that\r\nsupreme quality left to bestow. So far the obvious thing was not to\r\nentertain any idea of marriage with Thomasin, even to oblige her.\r\n\r\nBut this was not all. Years ago there had been in his mother\xe2\x80\x99s mind a\r\ngreat fancy about Thomasin and himself. It had not positively amounted\r\nto a desire, but it had always been a favourite dream. That they should\r\nbe man and wife in good time, if the happiness of neither were\r\nendangered thereby, was the fancy in question. So that what course save\r\none was there now left for any son who reverenced his mother\xe2\x80\x99s memory\r\nas Yeobright did? It is an unfortunate fact that any particular whim of\r\nparents, which might have been dispersed by half an hour\xe2\x80\x99s conversation\r\nduring their lives, becomes sublimated by their deaths into a fiat the\r\nmost absolute, with such results to conscientious children as those\r\nparents, had they lived, would have been the first to decry.\r\n\r\nHad only Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s own future been involved he would have proposed to\r\nThomasin with a ready heart. He had nothing to lose by carrying out a\r\ndead mother\xe2\x80\x99s hope. But he dreaded to contemplate Thomasin wedded to\r\nthe mere corpse of a lover that he now felt himself to be. He had but\r\nthree activities alive in him. One was his almost daily walk to the\r\nlittle graveyard wherein his mother lay; another, his just as frequent\r\nvisits by night to the more distant enclosure which numbered his\r\nEustacia among its dead; the third was self-preparation for a vocation\r\nwhich alone seemed likely to satisfy his cravings\xe2\x80\x94that of an itinerant\r\npreacher of the eleventh commandment. It was difficult to believe that\r\nThomasin would be cheered by a husband with such tendencies as these.\r\n\r\nYet he resolved to ask her, and let her decide for herself. It was even\r\nwith a pleasant sense of doing his duty that he went downstairs to her\r\none evening for this purpose, when the sun was printing on the valley\r\nthe same long shadow of the housetop that he had seen lying there times\r\nout of number while his mother lived.\r\n\r\nThomasin was not in her room, and he found her in the front garden. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave long been wanting, Thomasin,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, \xe2\x80\x9cto say something about a\r\nmatter that concerns both our futures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are going to say it now?\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked quickly, colouring as\r\nshe met his gaze. \xe2\x80\x9cDo stop a minute, Clym, and let me speak first, for\r\noddly enough, I have been wanting to say something to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy all means say on, Tamsie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose nobody can overhear us?\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, casting her eyes\r\naround and lowering her voice. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, first you will promise me\r\nthis\xe2\x80\x94that you won\xe2\x80\x99t be angry and call me anything harsh if you disagree\r\nwith what I propose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYeobright promised, and she continued: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat I want is your advice, for\r\nyou are my relation\xe2\x80\x94I mean, a sort of guardian to me\xe2\x80\x94aren\xe2\x80\x99t you, Clym?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes, I suppose I am; a sort of guardian. In fact, I am, of\r\ncourse,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, altogether perplexed as to her drift.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am thinking of marrying,\xe2\x80\x9d she then observed blandly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I shall\r\nnot marry unless you assure me that you approve of such a step. Why\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you speak?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was taken rather by surprise. But, nevertheless, I am very glad to\r\nhear such news. I shall approve, of course, dear Tamsie. Who can it be?\r\nI am quite at a loss to guess. No I am not\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis the old doctor!\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nthat I mean to call him old, for he is not very old after all. Ah\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nnoticed when he attended you last time!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d she said hastily. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis Mr. Venn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym\xe2\x80\x99s face suddenly became grave.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, now, you don\xe2\x80\x99t like him, and I wish I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t mentioned him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe exclaimed almost petulantly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done it, either,\r\nonly he keeps on bothering me so till I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym looked at the heath. \xe2\x80\x9cI like Venn well enough,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered at\r\nlast. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is a very honest and at the same time astute man. He is\r\nclever too, as is proved by his having got you to favour him. But\r\nreally, Thomasin, he is not quite\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGentleman enough for me? That is just what I feel. I am sorry now that\r\nI asked you, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t think any more of him. At the same time I must\r\nmarry him if I marry anybody\xe2\x80\x94that I _will_ say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym, carefully concealing every clue to his\r\nown interrupted intention, which she plainly had not guessed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nmight marry a professional man, or somebody of that sort, by going into\r\nthe town to live and forming acquaintances there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not fit for town life\xe2\x80\x94so very rural and silly as I always have\r\nbeen. Do not you yourself notice my countrified ways?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, when I came home from Paris I did, a little; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s because you have got countrified too. O, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t live in a\r\nstreet for the world! Egdon is a ridiculous old place; but I have got\r\nused to it, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be happy anywhere else at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither could I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen how could you say that I should marry some town man? I am sure,\r\nsay what you will, that I must marry Diggory, if I marry at all. He has\r\nbeen kinder to me than anybody else, and has helped me in many ways\r\nthat I don\xe2\x80\x99t know of!\xe2\x80\x9d Thomasin almost pouted now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he has,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym in a neutral tone. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wish with all my\r\nheart that I could say, marry him. But I cannot forget what my mother\r\nthought on that matter, and it goes rather against me not to respect\r\nher opinion. There is too much reason why we should do the little we\r\ncan to respect it now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Thomasin. \xe2\x80\x9cI will say no more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are not bound to obey my wishes. I merely say what I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO no\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be rebellious in that way,\xe2\x80\x9d she said sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhad no business to think of him\xe2\x80\x94I ought to have thought of my family.\r\nWhat dreadfully bad impulses there are in me!\xe2\x80\x9d Her lips trembled, and\r\nshe turned away to hide a tear.\r\n\r\nClym, though vexed at what seemed her unaccountable taste, was in a\r\nmeasure relieved to find that at any rate the marriage question in\r\nrelation to himself was shelved. Through several succeeding days he saw\r\nher at different times from the window of his room moping\r\ndisconsolately about the garden. He was half angry with her for\r\nchoosing Venn; then he was grieved at having put himself in the way of\r\nVenn\xe2\x80\x99s happiness, who was, after all, as honest and persevering a young\r\nfellow as any on Egdon, since he had turned over a new leaf. In short,\r\nClym did not know what to do.\r\n\r\nWhen next they met she said abruptly, \xe2\x80\x9cHe is much more respectable now\r\nthan he was then!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? O yes\xe2\x80\x94Diggory Venn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAunt only objected because he was a reddleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Thomasin, perhaps I don\xe2\x80\x99t know all the particulars of my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s wish. So you had better use your own discretion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will always feel that I slighted your mother\xe2\x80\x99s memory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I will not. I shall think you are convinced that, had she seen\r\nDiggory in his present position, she would have considered him a\r\nfitting husband for you. Now, that\xe2\x80\x99s my real feeling. Don\xe2\x80\x99t consult me\r\nany more, but do as you like, Thomasin. I shall be content.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt is to be supposed that Thomasin was convinced; for a few days after\r\nthis, when Clym strayed into a part of the heath that he had not lately\r\nvisited, Humphrey, who was at work there, said to him, \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to\r\nsee that Mrs. Wildeve and Venn have made it up again, seemingly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym abstractedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and he do contrive to stumble upon her whenever she walks out on\r\nfine days with the chiel. But, Mr. Yeobright, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help feeling that\r\nyour cousin ought to have married you. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a pity to make two\r\nchimleycorners where there need be only one. You could get her away\r\nfrom him now, \xe2\x80\x99tis my belief, if you were only to set about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I have the conscience to marry after having driven two women\r\nto their deaths? Don\xe2\x80\x99t think such a thing, Humphrey. After my\r\nexperience I should consider it too much of a burlesque to go to church\r\nand take a wife. In the words of Job, \xe2\x80\x98I have made a covenant with mine\r\neyes; when then should I think upon a maid?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Mr. Clym, don\xe2\x80\x99t fancy that about driving two women to their\r\ndeaths. You shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we\xe2\x80\x99ll leave that out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright. \xe2\x80\x9cBut anyhow God has set a\r\nmark upon me which wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t look well in a love-making scene. I have\r\ntwo ideas in my head, and no others. I am going to keep a night-school;\r\nand I am going to turn preacher. What have you got to say to that,\r\nHumphrey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll come and hear \xe2\x80\x99ee with all my heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks. \xe2\x80\x99Tis all I wish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Clym descended into the valley Thomasin came down by the other path,\r\nand met him at the gate. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think I have to tell you, Clym?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said, looking archly over her shoulder at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can guess,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\nShe scrutinized his face. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, you guess right. It is going to be\r\nafter all. He thinks I may as well make up my mind, and I have got to\r\nthink so too. It is to be on the twenty-fifth of next month, if you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t object.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo what you think right, dear. I am only too glad that you see your\r\nway clear to happiness again. My sex owes you every amends for the\r\ntreatment you received in days gone by.\xe2\x80\x9d*\r\n\r\n [*] The writer may state here that the original conception of the\r\n story did not design a marriage between Thomasin and Venn. He was to\r\n have retained his isolated and weird character to the last, and to\r\n have disappeared mysteriously from the heath, nobody knowing\r\n whither\xe2\x80\x94Thomasin remaining a widow. But certain circumstances of\r\n serial publication led to a change of intent.\r\n    Readers can therefore choose between the endings, and those with an\r\n    austere artistic code can assume the more consistent conclusion to\r\n    be the true one.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIV.\r\nCheerfulness Again Asserts Itself at Blooms-End, and Clym Finds His\r\nVocation\r\n\r\n\r\nAnybody who had passed through Blooms-End about eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock on the\r\nmorning fixed for the wedding would have found that, while Yeobright\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse was comparatively quiet, sounds denoting great activity came from\r\nthe dwelling of his nearest neighbour, Timothy Fairway. It was chiefly\r\na noise of feet, briskly crunching hither and thither over the sanded\r\nfloor within. One man only was visible outside, and he seemed to be\r\nlater at an appointment than he had intended to be, for he hastened up\r\nto the door, lifted the latch, and walked in without ceremony.\r\n\r\nThe scene within was not quite the customary one. Standing about the\r\nroom was the little knot of men who formed the chief part of the Egdon\r\ncoterie, there being present Fairway himself, Grandfer Cantle,\r\nHumphrey, Christian, and one or two turf-cutters. It was a warm day,\r\nand the men were as a matter of course in their shirtsleeves, except\r\nChristian, who had always a nervous fear of parting with a scrap of his\r\nclothing when in anybody\xe2\x80\x99s house but his own. Across the stout oak\r\ntable in the middle of the room was thrown a mass of striped linen,\r\nwhich Grandfer Cantle held down on one side, and Humphrey on the other,\r\nwhile Fairway rubbed its surface with a yellow lump, his face being\r\ndamp and creased with the effort of the labour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWaxing a bed-tick, souls?\xe2\x80\x9d said the newcomer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Sam,\xe2\x80\x9d said Grandfer Cantle, as a man too busy to waste words.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I stretch this corner a shade tighter, Timothy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFairway replied, and the waxing went on with unabated vigour. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis\r\ngoing to be a good bed, by the look o\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Sam, after an\r\ninterval of silence. \xe2\x80\x9cWho may it be for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a present for the new folks that\xe2\x80\x99s going to set up housekeeping,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Christian, who stood helpless and overcome by the majesty of the\r\nproceedings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, to be sure; and a valuable one, \xe2\x80\x99a b\xe2\x80\x99lieve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeds be dear to fokes that don\xe2\x80\x99t keep geese, bain\xe2\x80\x99t they, Mister\r\nFairway?\xe2\x80\x9d said Christian, as to an omniscient being.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the furze-dealer, standing up, giving his forehead a\r\nthorough mopping, and handing the beeswax to Humphrey, who succeeded at\r\nthe rubbing forthwith. \xe2\x80\x9cNot that this couple be in want of one, but\r\n\xe2\x80\x99twas well to show \xe2\x80\x99em a bit of friendliness at this great racketing\r\nvagary of their lives. I set up both my own daughters in one when they\r\nwas married, and there have been feathers enough for another in the\r\nhouse the last twelve months. Now then, neighbours, I think we have\r\nlaid on enough wax. Grandfer Cantle, you turn the tick the right way\r\noutwards, and then I\xe2\x80\x99ll begin to shake in the feathers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the bed was in proper trim Fairway and Christian brought forward\r\nvast paper bags, stuffed to the full, but light as balloons, and began\r\nto turn the contents of each into the receptacle just prepared. As bag\r\nafter bag was emptied, airy tufts of down and feathers floated about\r\nthe room in increasing quantity till, through a mishap of Christian\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nwho shook the contents of one bag outside the tick, the atmosphere of\r\nthe room became dense with gigantic flakes, which descended upon the\r\nworkers like a windless snowstorm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never saw such a clumsy chap as you, Christian,\xe2\x80\x9d said Grandfer\r\nCantle severely. \xe2\x80\x9cYou might have been the son of a man that\xe2\x80\x99s never\r\nbeen outside Blooms-End in his life for all the wit you have. Really\r\nall the soldiering and smartness in the world in the father seems to\r\ncount for nothing in forming the nater of the son. As far as that chief\r\nChristian is concerned I might as well have stayed at home and seed\r\nnothing, like all the rest of ye here. Though, as far as myself is\r\nconcerned, a dashing spirit has counted for sommat, to be sure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ye let me down so, Father; I feel no bigger than a ninepin after\r\nit. I\xe2\x80\x99ve made but a bruckle hit, I\xe2\x80\x99m afeard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come. Never pitch yerself in such a low key as that, Christian;\r\nyou should try more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you should try more,\xe2\x80\x9d echoed the Grandfer with insistence, as if\r\nhe had been the first to make the suggestion. \xe2\x80\x9cIn common conscience\r\nevery man ought either to marry or go for a soldier. \xe2\x80\x99Tis a scandal to\r\nthe nation to do neither one nor t\xe2\x80\x99other. I did both, thank God!\r\nNeither to raise men nor to lay \xe2\x80\x99em low\xe2\x80\x94that shows a poor do-nothing\r\nspirit indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never had the nerve to stand fire,\xe2\x80\x9d faltered Christian. \xe2\x80\x9cBut as to\r\nmarrying, I own I\xe2\x80\x99ve asked here and there, though without much fruit\r\nfrom it. Yes, there\xe2\x80\x99s some house or other that might have had a man for\r\na master\xe2\x80\x94such as he is\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s now ruled by a woman alone. Still it\r\nmight have been awkward if I had found her; for, d\xe2\x80\x99ye see, neighbours,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99d have been nobody left at home to keep down Father\xe2\x80\x99s spirits to\r\nthe decent pitch that becomes a old man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you\xe2\x80\x99ve your work cut out to do that, my son,\xe2\x80\x9d said Grandfer Cantle\r\nsmartly. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish that the dread of infirmities was not so strong in\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99d start the very first thing tomorrow to see the world over\r\nagain! But seventy-one, though nothing at home, is a high figure for a\r\nrover.... Ay, seventy-one, last Candlemasday. Gad, I\xe2\x80\x99d sooner have it\r\nin guineas than in years!\xe2\x80\x9d And the old man sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you be mournful, Grandfer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway. \xe2\x80\x9cEmpt some more\r\nfeathers into the bed-tick, and keep up yer heart. Though rather lean\r\nin the stalks you be a green-leaved old man still. There\xe2\x80\x99s time enough\r\nleft to ye yet to fill whole chronicles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBegad, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to \xe2\x80\x99em, Timothy\xe2\x80\x94to the married pair!\xe2\x80\x9d said Granfer\r\nCantle in an encouraged voice, and starting round briskly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go to\r\n\xe2\x80\x99em tonight and sing a wedding song, hey? \xe2\x80\x99Tis like me to do so, you\r\nknow; and they\xe2\x80\x99d see it as such. My \xe2\x80\x98Down in Cupid\xe2\x80\x99s Gardens\xe2\x80\x99 was well\r\nliked in four; still, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got others as good, and even better. What do\r\nyou say to my\r\n\r\nShe cal\xe2\x80\xb2-led to\xe2\x80\xb2 her love\xe2\x80\xb2\r\nFrom the lat\xe2\x80\xb2-tice a-bove,\r\n\xe2\x80\xb2O come in\xe2\x80\xb2 from the fog-gy fog\xe2\x80\xb2-gy dew\xe2\x80\xb2.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Twould please \xe2\x80\x99em well at such a time! Really, now I come to think of\r\nit, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t turned my tongue in my head to the shape of a real good\r\nsong since Old Midsummer night, when we had the \xe2\x80\x98Barley Mow\xe2\x80\x99 at the\r\nWoman; and \xe2\x80\x99tis a pity to neglect your strong point where there\xe2\x80\x99s few\r\nthat have the compass for such things!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo \xe2\x80\x99tis, so \xe2\x80\x99tis,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway. \xe2\x80\x9cNow gie the bed a shake down. We\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nput in seventy pounds of best feathers, and I think that\xe2\x80\x99s as many as\r\nthe tick will fairly hold. A bit and a drap wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be amiss now, I\r\nreckon. Christian, maul down the victuals from corner-cupboard if canst\r\nreach, man, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll draw a drap o\xe2\x80\x99 sommat to wet it with.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey sat down to a lunch in the midst of their work, feathers around,\r\nabove, and below them; the original owners of which occasionally came\r\nto the open door and cackled begrudgingly at sight of such a quantity\r\nof their old clothes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my soul I shall be chokt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway when, having extracted a\r\nfeather from his mouth, he found several others floating on the mug as\r\nit was handed round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve swallered several; and one had a tolerable quill,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sam\r\nplacidly from the corner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s that\xe2\x80\x94wheels I hear coming?\xe2\x80\x9d Grandfer Cantle exclaimed,\r\njumping up and hastening to the door. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, \xe2\x80\x99tis they back again\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t expect \xe2\x80\x99em yet this half-hour. To be sure, how quick marrying\r\ncan be done when you are in the mind for\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO yes, it can soon be _done_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fairway, as if something should be\r\nadded to make the statement complete.\r\n\r\nHe arose and followed the Grandfer, and the rest also went to the door.\r\nIn a moment an open fly was driven past, in which sat Venn and Mrs.\r\nVenn, Yeobright, and a grand relative of Venn\xe2\x80\x99s who had come from\r\nBudmouth for the occasion. The fly had been hired at the nearest town,\r\nregardless of distance and cost, there being nothing on Egdon Heath, in\r\nVenn\xe2\x80\x99s opinion, dignified enough for such an event when such a woman as\r\nThomasin was the bride; and the church was too remote for a walking\r\nbridal-party.\r\n\r\nAs the fly passed the group which had run out from the homestead they\r\nshouted \xe2\x80\x9cHurrah!\xe2\x80\x9d and waved their hands; feathers and down floating\r\nfrom their hair, their sleeves, and the folds of their garments at\r\nevery motion, and Grandfer Cantle\xe2\x80\x99s seals dancing merrily in the\r\nsunlight as he twirled himself about. The driver of the fly turned a\r\nsupercilious gaze upon them; he even treated the wedded pair themselves\r\nwith something like condescension; for in what other state than heathen\r\ncould people, rich or poor, exist who were doomed to abide in such a\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x99s end as Egdon? Thomasin showed no such superiority to the group\r\nat the door, fluttering her hand as quickly as a bird\xe2\x80\x99s wing towards\r\nthem, and asking Diggory, with tears in her eyes, if they ought not to\r\nalight and speak to these kind neighbours. Venn, however, suggested\r\nthat, as they were all coming to the house in the evening, this was\r\nhardly necessary.\r\n\r\nAfter this excitement the saluting party returned to their occupation,\r\nand the stuffing and sewing were soon afterwards finished, when Fairway\r\nharnessed a horse, wrapped up the cumbrous present, and drove off with\r\nit in the cart to Venn\xe2\x80\x99s house at Stickleford.\r\n\r\nYeobright, having filled the office at the wedding service which\r\nnaturally fell to his hands, and afterwards returned to the house with\r\nthe husband and wife, was indisposed to take part in the feasting and\r\ndancing that wound up the evening. Thomasin was disappointed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could be there without dashing your spirits,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\nmight be too much like the skull at the banquet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dear, apart from that, if you would excuse me, I should be glad.\r\nI know it seems unkind; but, dear Thomasin, I fear I should not be\r\nhappy in the company\xe2\x80\x94there, that\xe2\x80\x99s the truth of it. I shall always be\r\ncoming to see you at your new home, you know, so that my absence now\r\nwill not matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I give in. Do whatever will be most comfortable to yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClym retired to his lodging at the housetop much relieved, and occupied\r\nhimself during the afternoon in noting down the heads of a sermon, with\r\nwhich he intended to initiate all that really seemed practicable of the\r\nscheme that had originally brought him hither, and that he had so long\r\nkept in view under various modifications, and through evil and good\r\nreport. He had tested and weighed his convictions again and again, and\r\nsaw no reason to alter them, though he had considerably lessened his\r\nplan. His eyesight, by long humouring in his native air, had grown\r\nstronger, but not sufficiently strong to warrant his attempting his\r\nextensive educational project. Yet he did not repine\xe2\x80\x94there was still\r\nmore than enough of an unambitious sort to tax all his energies and\r\noccupy all his hours.\r\n\r\nEvening drew on, and sounds of life and movement in the lower part of\r\nthe domicile became more pronounced, the gate in the palings clicking\r\nincessantly. The party was to be an early one, and all the guests were\r\nassembled long before it was dark. Yeobright went down the back\r\nstaircase and into the heath by another path than that in front,\r\nintending to walk in the open air till the party was over, when he\r\nwould return to wish Thomasin and her husband good-bye as they\r\ndeparted. His steps were insensibly bent towards Mistover by the path\r\nthat he had followed on that terrible morning when he learnt the\r\nstrange news from Susan\xe2\x80\x99s boy.\r\n\r\nHe did not turn aside to the cottage, but pushed on to an eminence,\r\nwhence he could see over the whole quarter that had once been\r\nEustacia\xe2\x80\x99s home. While he stood observing the darkening scene somebody\r\ncame up. Clym, seeing him but dimly, would have let him pass silently,\r\nhad not the pedestrian, who was Charley, recognized the young man and\r\nspoken to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharley, I have not seen you for a length of time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Yeobright.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you often walk this way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d the lad replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t often come outside the bank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were not at the Maypole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charley, in the same listless tone. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care for that\r\nsort of thing now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou rather liked Miss Eustacia, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d Yeobright gently asked.\r\nEustacia had frequently told him of Charley\xe2\x80\x99s romantic attachment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, very much. Ah, I wish\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish, Mr. Yeobright, you could give me something to keep that once\r\nbelonged to her\xe2\x80\x94if you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be very happy to. It will give me very great pleasure,\r\nCharley. Let me think what I have of hers that you would like. But come\r\nwith me to the house, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey walked towards Blooms-End together. When they reached the front it\r\nwas dark, and the shutters were closed, so that nothing of the interior\r\ncould be seen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome round this way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym. \xe2\x80\x9cMy entrance is at the back for the\r\npresent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two went round and ascended the crooked stair in darkness till\r\nClym\xe2\x80\x99s sitting-room on the upper floor was reached, where he lit a\r\ncandle, Charley entering gently behind. Yeobright searched his desk,\r\nand taking out a sheet of tissue-paper unfolded from it two or three\r\nundulating locks of raven hair, which fell over the paper like black\r\nstreams. From these he selected one, wrapped it up, and gave it to the\r\nlad, whose eyes had filled with tears. He kissed the packet, put it in\r\nhis pocket, and said in a voice of emotion, \xe2\x80\x9cO, Mr. Clym, how good you\r\nare to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go a little way with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym. And amid the noise of\r\nmerriment from below they descended. Their path to the front led them\r\nclose to a little side window, whence the rays of candles streamed\r\nacross the shrubs. The window, being screened from general observation\r\nby the bushes, had been left unblinded, so that a person in this\r\nprivate nook could see all that was going on within the room which\r\ncontained the wedding guests, except in so far as vision was hindered\r\nby the green antiquity of the panes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharley, what are they doing?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym. \xe2\x80\x9cMy sight is weaker again\r\ntonight, and the glass of this window is not good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharley wiped his own eyes, which were rather blurred with moisture,\r\nand stepped closer to the casement. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Venn is asking Christian\r\nCantle to sing,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, \xe2\x80\x9cand Christian is moving about in his\r\nchair as if he were much frightened at the question, and his father has\r\nstruck up a stave instead of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I can hear the old man\xe2\x80\x99s voice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym. \xe2\x80\x9cSo there\xe2\x80\x99s to be no\r\ndancing, I suppose. And is Thomasin in the room? I see something moving\r\nin front of the candles that resembles her shape, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. She do seem happy. She is red in the face, and laughing at\r\nsomething Fairway has said to her. O my!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat noise was that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Venn is so tall that he knocked his head against the beam in\r\ngieing a skip as he passed under. Mrs. Venn has run up quite frightened\r\nand now she\xe2\x80\x99s put her hand to his head to feel if there\xe2\x80\x99s a lump. And\r\nnow they be all laughing again as if nothing had happened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo any of them seem to care about my not being there?\xe2\x80\x9d Clym asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not a bit in the world. Now they are all holding up their glasses\r\nand drinking somebody\xe2\x80\x99s health.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if it is mine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, \xe2\x80\x99tis Mr. and Mrs. Venn\xe2\x80\x99s, because he is making a hearty sort of\r\nspeech. There\xe2\x80\x94now Mrs. Venn has got up, and is going away to put on her\r\nthings, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they haven\xe2\x80\x99t concerned themselves about me, and it is quite\r\nright they should not. It is all as it should be, and Thomasin at least\r\nis happy. We will not stay any longer now, as they will soon be coming\r\nout to go home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe accompanied the lad into the heath on his way home, and, returning\r\nalone to the house a quarter of an hour later, found Venn and Thomasin\r\nready to start, all the guests having departed in his absence. The\r\nwedded pair took their seats in the four-wheeled dogcart which Venn\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhead milker and handy man had driven from Stickleford to fetch them in;\r\nlittle Eustacia and the nurse were packed securely upon the open flap\r\nbehind; and the milker, on an ancient overstepping pony, whose shoes\r\nclashed like cymbals at every tread, rode in the rear, in the manner of\r\na body-servant of the last century.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we leave you in absolute possession of your own house again,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nThomasin as she bent down to wish her cousin good night. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will be\r\nrather lonely for you, Clym, after the hubbub we have been making.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, that\xe2\x80\x99s no inconvenience,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clym, smiling rather sadly. And then\r\nthe party drove off and vanished in the night shades, and Yeobright\r\nentered the house. The ticking of the clock was the only sound that\r\ngreeted him, for not a soul remained; Christian, who acted as cook,\r\nvalet, and gardener to Clym, sleeping at his father\xe2\x80\x99s house. Yeobright\r\nsat down in one of the vacant chairs, and remained in thought a long\r\ntime. His mother\xe2\x80\x99s old chair was opposite; it had been sat in that\r\nevening by those who had scarcely remembered that it ever was hers. But\r\nto Clym she was almost a presence there, now as always. Whatever she\r\nwas in other people\xe2\x80\x99s memories, in his she was the sublime saint whose\r\nradiance even his tenderness for Eustacia could not obscure. But his\r\nheart was heavy, that Mother had _not_ crowned him in the day of his\r\nespousals and in the day of the gladness of his heart. And events had\r\nborne out the accuracy of her judgment, and proved the devotedness of\r\nher care. He should have heeded her for Eustacia\xe2\x80\x99s sake even more than\r\nfor his own. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was all my fault,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cO, my mother, my\r\nmother! would to God that I could live my life again, and endure for\r\nyou what you endured for me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the Sunday after this wedding an unusual sight was to be seen on\r\nRainbarrow. From a distance there simply appeared to be a motionless\r\nfigure standing on the top of the tumulus, just as Eustacia had stood\r\non that lonely summit some two years and a half before. But now it was\r\nfine warm weather, with only a summer breeze blowing, and early\r\nafternoon instead of dull twilight. Those who ascended to the immediate\r\nneighbourhood of the Barrow perceived that the erect form in the\r\ncentre, piercing the sky, was not really alone. Round him upon the\r\nslopes of the Barrow a number of heathmen and women were reclining or\r\nsitting at their ease. They listened to the words of the man in their\r\nmidst, who was preaching, while they abstractedly pulled heather,\r\nstripped ferns, or tossed pebbles down the slope. This was the first of\r\na series of moral lectures or Sermons on the Mount, which were to be\r\ndelivered from the same place every Sunday afternoon as long as the\r\nfine weather lasted.\r\n\r\nThe commanding elevation of Rainbarrow had been chosen for two reasons:\r\nfirst, that it occupied a central position among the remote cottages\r\naround; secondly, that the preacher thereon could be seen from all\r\nadjacent points as soon as he arrived at his post, the view of him\r\nbeing thus a convenient signal to those stragglers who wished to draw\r\nnear. The speaker was bareheaded, and the breeze at each waft gently\r\nlifted and lowered his hair, somewhat too thin for a man of his years,\r\nthese still numbering less than thirty-three. He wore a shade over his\r\neyes, and his face was pensive and lined; but, though these bodily\r\nfeatures were marked with decay there was no defect in the tones of his\r\nvoice, which were rich, musical, and stirring. He stated that his\r\ndiscourses to people were to be sometimes secular, and sometimes\r\nreligious, but never dogmatic; and that his texts would be taken from\r\nall kinds of books. This afternoon the words were as follows:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98And the king rose up to meet her, and bowed himself unto her, and sat\r\ndown on his throne, and caused a seat to be set for the king\xe2\x80\x99s mother;\r\nand she sat on his right hand. Then she said, I desire one small\r\npetition of thee; I pray thee say me not nay. And the king said unto\r\nher, Ask, on, my mother: for I will not say thee nay.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYeobright had, in fact, found his vocation in the career of an\r\nitinerant open-air preacher and lecturer on morally unimpeachable\r\nsubjects; and from this day he laboured incessantly in that office,\r\nspeaking not only in simple language on Rainbarrow and in the hamlets\r\nround, but in a more cultivated strain elsewhere\xe2\x80\x94from the steps and\r\nporticoes of town halls, from market-crosses, from conduits, on\r\nesplanades and on wharves, from the parapets of bridges, in barns and\r\nouthouses, and all other such places in the neighbouring Wessex towns\r\nand villages. He left alone creeds and systems of philosophy, finding\r\nenough and more than enough to occupy his tongue in the opinions and\r\nactions common to all good men. Some believed him, and some believed\r\nnot; some said that his words were commonplace, others complained of\r\nhis want of theological doctrine; while others again remarked that it\r\nwas well enough for a man to take to preaching who could not see to do\r\nanything else. But everywhere he was kindly received, for the story of\r\nhis life had become generally known.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE RETURN OF THE NATIVE ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 122-0.txt or 122-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/1/2/122/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
